Onleadbig Terms:    The proceeds of the loan, except $2.3 million for technical assistance
to the Government of Indonesia (GOD, will be onlent from GOI to
PLN for 20 years, including a grace period of 5 years; the onlending
rate will be equal to the Bank's standard variable fee equal to the
average of the annual rate of change in the value of Rupiah during the
previous three years, with respect to US dollar, plus one percent. The
Government would bear the foreign exchange risk.
Pro -t
De.  dion:         The main objective of the project is to provide economical and
environmentally sound expansion of PLN's peaking electricity
generating capacity in Java, and improve service reliability. Its
components are:  (a) inplementation of Phase II of the Cirata
hydroelecttic development on the Citarum River in West Java,
consisting of:  (i) insllaion, within the existing cavern of the
powerhouse of four additional units each of 125 MW, including
turbines, generators, governors, regulator and control equipment; (ii)
constructionof two headrace tunnels, two surge shafts, four steel lined
pemtock tunnels, and four tailrace tunnels, with associated outlet
structures; and (iii) installion of transformers and switchgear for
connection to the grid; (b) consulting services for the eineering and
construction supervision of the Cirata Hydroelectric Phase II
development; (c) studies to upgrade hydrological and environmental
data bases, and improve data collection practices; (d) studies to update
PLN's hydroelectric inventories, and determine pre-feasibility of
selected schemes; (e) studies to identify options to enhance
environmental conditions in the existing PLN reservoirs on the Citarum
River, and training of PLN staff in environmental management;
(f) training of PLN's staff in dam safety and instrumentation
monitoring; (g) power sector institutional and regulatory framework
studies for GOI; (h) studies aimed at assisting GOI to determine a long-
This document has a restricted distibution and may be used by recipients Only in the performance
of their olllcial duties Its contents may not otherwise be disclosed without World Bank authorization.



tam strategy for financing power sector investments; and (i) pre-
consucton stage engineering services for the Besai hydroelectric
project in South Sumatra.
Bemb:               The project would help PLN meet inrease in peak demand for
electricity in Java and improve service reliability. The project would
also help strenghen PLN's financial position by enhancing the dialogue
on the introduction of a nechanisn for the periodic adjustment of
electricity tariffs, improve PLN's instiutional capabilities, and achieve
greater efficiency in the power subsector by promoting reform in the
institutional and regulatory framework in the sector to empower public
and private sector institutions in the sector to perform to their full
potetials.
Rsk:                The main risks of project imple ion relate to potental cost and
schedule overruns due to geological and hydrological unertainties, and
conactor and construction supervision inadeuacies. The geological
risb are minor because of the knowledge gained in the construction of
the Cirata Phase I development, extensive investigations of new tunnel
alignments, and the fact that the undeground power cavern is already
complete. Hydrological uncertainties are low, since only smal work
is to be done in the river down stream of the dam where flood risk is
negligible. Appointment of a fully qualified civil works contror
trough a prqualification process, use of experienced international
conultants in the construction supervision process, and PLN's
aointment of a project manager for the Cirata Phase II development
would reduce project management risks to a minimum. There is little
risk that the proposed project would become redundt if the projected
level of electricity demand does not matrialize, since the project is a
part of the core expansion program of PLN and is justified under all
demand scenarios considered. Overall, there is a risk that the planned
large esion program for the sector may not be carried out for lack
of sufficient financing and/or implementation capacity, which may
result in supply shortages and consequent disruptions in the national
economy. This risk is addressed through Bank-spported measures
aimng at enhancing sector efficiency through changes in the
ituonal and regulatory framework of the sector, as well as in the
organitional structure of PLN, which would enable the public and the
private sectors (including coperatives) to apply their full potential
towards meeting the technical, managerl and financial r irements
of the sector.

TEENERGY SECTOR
1.1       Indonesia is endowed with a large and dir  energy resource base including crude oil,
natural gas, coal, bydropower and geothemal. Oil has played a cricial role in the country's development.
In the early 1980s, oil's iwportance in the Indonesian economy peaked as oil accounted for 80 percent
of the country's export earnings, and most of the governmnt's revenues came from oil. Pollowing the
collapse of oil prices in the first half of the 1980s, Indonesia inidated a series of fundamental reforms to
restucture and diversify the economy, and provided incentives for non-oil/LNG m a   and
exports; the latter amounted to about US$19.0 billion in 1991/92. However, the oil and gas sector
conine to retain a fudamental role in Indonesia's economy, with oillLNG exports providing revenues
of about US$10.7 billion in 1991/92, constitting roughly 36 percent of total exports.
1.2       Indonesia's domesdc energy consumption has been growing rapidly in recent years. Overall
domestic net energy use grew from about 166 million barrels of oil equivalent (boe) in 1985 to about 232
million boe in 1990, a compound rate of 6.8 per cent per anmum; with liquid petroleum products
accountg for about 78 percent of net energy consumed, and the share of natural gas at approximately
10 percent. Liquid petroleum product co_nspon is curreny increasing at about 13 percent anmually
after having grown at an average annual rate of about 6 percent from 1986 to 1989. During the 1980s,
electricity conumption grew about 15 percent annually and gas sales grew about 9 percent annually.
1.3       A primary objective of energy policy in Indonesia is to satisfy the rapidly growing demand
for energy while mnnimizing the diversion of liquid petroleum products from exports. The Govenmnent
is conscious of the need to conserve Indonesia's exportable surplus of crude oil and thereby slow
Indonesa's transition to net oil importer stus. This requirs the efficient use of energy resources to be
acomplished trdough efficiency pricing and other means, and the development of alternative energy
resources that can economically substiute for domestic petroleum use, such as coal, non exportable gas,
geothrm  and hydropower. In the power sector, a major user of petroleum fuels, plans call for fi-ther
reducing oil's share of power generation, from about 44 percent in 1991/92 ( about 33 million barrels
of diesel and fuel oil), to about 5 percent by the end of this decade (10 million barrels per year).



-2-
Sector Overview
1.4      0Ol.  Indonesia's proven and potental reseves of oil have been estimated to be about 11
billion barrels as of January 1991, of whidh proven reserves are estimated to be 6 billion barrels. The
production of crude oil and cotdensates in 1991 was about 581 million barrels, which implies a reserves-
to-production ratio of 19:1. The World Bank's medium term projections indicate tbal whereas in volume
terms Indonesia would still appear to be a comfortable net exporter of oil through this decade, in value
terms it could become a net oil importer well before the end of this century. The major reasons for the
decline are the rapid growth in domestic product demands and the growing imporation of such petroleum
products, as welH as the cost recovery and factor service payments which have to be made to oil
contractors.
1.5       The Bank's participation in the development of the oil sector has been primarily through its
dialogue on energy pricing issues. The Bank has being advocating the use of efficiency pricing as the
basic guideline for demmining petroleum product prices under Indonesia's administered pricng approach.
An appropriate guidelme for adjusting domestic fuel prices is the world market price for refined
petroleum products with selective departures from this benchmark for reveme and equity reasons.
President Suharto's Budget Speech in January 1993, enunciated the principle that energy should be treaed
"as an ordinary commodity with the going price at the international market", and in the price adjustments
announced shordy thereafter, the Government has raised overall product prices by about 24 percent, and
elinated the overall subsidy that existed prior to the mcrease. With these incrases in product pries
that became effective on Januwy 8, 1993, only kerosene retains a sizeable subsidy, equivalent to about
30 percent of its efficiency price. All other product prices are at or above interational price levels, which
more than compesate for the subsidy on kemsene.
1.6       Natural Gas. Indonesia's indicative geological reserves of naual gas are estimated to be
about 217 trillion cubic feet (T), of which 65 TCF are classified as proven, and 39 TCF classified as
potential reserves. Among the proven and potential reserves there are about 8.4 TCF recoverable reserves
in structures too smali to support LNG exports, but large enough to be exploited for the domestic market.
It is estimated that firther exploration could result in the addition of 4 to 15 TCF to this category. The
current gross natura gas production is about 2.46 TCP (424 million boe), implying a reserve-to-
production ratio of 42:1, which is high by international standards.
1.7       Production of LNG exports contine to rise, rsondig to strong demand in the Far East.
In 1991192, LNG exports were valued at about US$3.8 billion (export volume of 19 million tons), and
represented about 81 percent of totl sales, with only 19 percent going to the domestic market, mainly
in fertilizer, iron and steel, and cement production. LNG demand in Japan, Korea, and Taiwan is
projected to expand in the 1990s by about 30.40 million tons, and Indomesia expects to have a significant
share of that market. Over the longer term, as oil's share of export revenmes decline, the Government's
expectation is that natural gas exports will compensate for the revemue losses.
1.8       Notwithstandig the acal and projected growth of the LNG export markets, the use of
natural gas for domestic energy consumption is becoming economically more attactive. Domestic
consumption of gas is projected to increase from 0.3 TCF in 1991 to over 1.0 TCF by the year 2000.



The largest increase in domestic gas conumption is projected to take place in the power sector, to supply
combined-cycle plants that represent a major element of the strategy to cut back on oil-based generation.
Plans call for increasing the share of natural gas-fired generation to about 22 percent by the end of this
decade (195 BCF), from 5 percent in 1991/92 (26 BCP).
1.9       The Gcvermment's strategy is to meet the projected domestic gas requirenents by developing
the non-exportable gas reserves and the necessary gas transmission infrastructure. The Bank has recently
completed a 'Natural Gas Development Planning Study" which provides an in depth assessment of the
issues and formulates a development strategy for the efficient domestic use of the resource. The findings
of the study were reviewed with the Government in June 1992. The Government's development strategy
is being refined in the context of preparing a second Gas Utilization Project. Bank loans are also helping
to finance Gas Distribution (Loan 2690-IND) and Gas Utilization (Loan 3290-IND) projects.
1.10      Coal. Indonesia's coal reserves are estimated to exceed 32 billion tons, located primarily
in Sumatra (23 billion tons) and Kaimantan (9 billion tons). The "measured reserves" are 4.2 billion
tons, divided nearly equally between Sumatra and Kalimantan.
1.11      Indonesia's anmnal production of coal has increased substially from about 600,00 tons in
1983 to over 15 million tons in 1992. Coal production is expected to exceed 30 million tons by 1994 and
could reach 50 million tons by 2000. Thus the rado of measured reserves to annual production is about
280:1. About 60 percent of the production at present comes from Sumatra and the remaining amount from
Kalimantan. Besides one state enterprise, a dozen foreign contractors share in joint ventures and in
firther exploration and mining activities.
1.12      In general Indonesian coal has low sulfur, low ash, high moisture, and high volatile matter.
The South Sunatra and Kali_antan coals have the potential for sustaining the large coal-fired power
generation program in the country that has been identified as part of the least-cost expansion plan for the
power sector. Under this plan, coal's share of power generation will increase from about 30 percent in
1991/92 ( about 5 million tons) to about 50 percent in 1999/00 (23 million tons). In addition to increasing
domestic consumption, exports of coal are also being developed, and Indonesia could become a
significant coal exporter in the coming years. In 1991, coal exports reached about 6.0 million tons and
an export capability of about 20 million tons is expected by 1994.
1.13      Geothenmal. The geothennal reserves of lndonesia are estimated at 10,000 MW, of which
5,500 MW is on Java. The first geothermal power plant with a capacity of 30 MW was built in 1976
at Kamojang near Bandung in West Java. The expansion of this plant to 140 MW was funded by the
Bank Loan 2214-IND. This geothermal field has potential for further expansion by 55 MW. Two more
power stations in Java, totalling 165 MW, are being constructed by PLN with financial assistance from
the Italian Govermment and the ADB respectively.
1.14      The main issue in the exploitation of geothermal potential is to locate sites that can be
developed at costs competitive with other options. In Java, where the electricity grid is well developed
and where PLN is able to achieve economies of scale by constucting large coal-fired or combined cycle
gas-fired stations, the geothermal option is not competitive at the current fuel prices. However, in the



-4 -
outer islands, where the cost of the alternatives are high, geothermal development would have greater
justification. PLN and (01 have, therefore, directed their priorties to developing geothermal sites
outside Java.
1.15      Hydropower. Indonesia has considerable hydroelectric resources, but their development
to date has been limited by their geographic distribution relative to the load centers where there is demand
for electricity. The greatest potential (over 35 percent) lies in Irian Jaya, which has less than one percent
of total electricity demand, while Java, which accounts for 80 percent of current electricity consumption,
has less than 10 percent of the total potential. In Java, about 2,000 MW of hydro potential has been
already developed, including two major projects viz. Saguling (750) MW) and Cirata (500 MW) funded
by Bank Loans 1950-IND and 2300-IND. The proposed project will add 500 MW of capacity to the
existng Cirata project. Hydroelectric plants outside Java totalling about 2,000 MW are also cuffently
under either planning or implementation.
1.16      Nuclear. Development of nuclear power in Indonesia has a lower priority compared to
power generation using natural gas and coal. However, in the long-term, when coal usage for power
generation becomes large enough to cause environmental concerns and when suitable sites for locating
coal-fired plants become difficult to find, nuclear technology could provide a viable alternative. The need
for a nuclear power plant is not envisaged until year 2005. The Government has recently commissioned
a feasibility study for a nuclear plant. This study will determine the opportune tining for the first nuclear
power plant and also recommend an appropriate technology.
Envfrnment
1.17        The Environment Law in Indonesia (Law No. 4, 1982) provides that every project likely
to have a significant impact on the environment must be accompanied by an environmental impact
analysis (ANDAL). Pursuant to this law, the Ministry of Mines and Energy, in its Decree 1158 (1989),
established uniform procedures and guidelines for preparation, submission and approval of an
enviromnental impact analysis, as well as subsequent and periodic reporting during the project
implementation phase. However, energy projects funded by different agencies do not follow a uniform
policy in respect of environmental standards. The Indonesian standards are not fully developed and the
capability of the Ministry of Mines and Energy for prepanng such standards is limited. Also, the
Ministry does not have adequate trained manpower or equipment to monitor and enforce environmental
regulations in operating facilities. Technical assistance was provided under Loan 3098-IND (Paiton
Thermal Power Project) to train a number of staff of the Ministry of Mines and Energy on environmental
management, and provide documentation for environmental guidelines and monitoring [para. 1.20(f)].
The TA was satisfactorily completed in October 1992. The proposed project includes technical assistance
to PLN for strengthening its capacity to undertake environmental studies and improve its monitoring
practices (para. 5.21).
Poverty Alleviation
1.18      The role of the energy sector in poverty alleviation is largely indirect except for the specific
government interventions through its pricing policies, involving subsidy for kerosene, low life-line rates



for use of electricity by small households and industries, and a uniform price structure for electricity
throughout the country. The rural electrification program is the most direct intervention of the sector in
the poverty alleviation effort. The Government, through PLN, is investing considerable resources to
expand electricity supply to the villages, generally at subsidized rates, since provision of electricity to
rural areas enhances the quality of life and permits the poor to have access to a service they value highly.
While the economic and social rationale of the rural electrification program is strong, measures to reduce
costs and promote local participation require special attention. In the Bank's sector report (Indonesia:
Rural Electrification Review, Report 6144-IND of November 1986), recommendations for such
improvements were made, which are being pursued through the ongoing Rural Electrification Project
(Loan 3180-ND). Potential actions center around the need to (i) address constraints that currendy inhibit
household connections, (ii) increase productive use of electricity, (iii) improve the village selection
criteria for electrification, (iv) improve physical system design and service standards to enhance cost
effectiveness, and (v) provide opportunity for the development of village-level managerial, technical,
labor, and entrepreneurial resources. A Second Rural Electrification Project, currently under preparation,
will build on the experience gained from the ongoing Rural Electrification Project (Loan 3180-IND).
The Goverment's Objectives and Strategy
1.19       In light of Indonesia's rich endowment in energy resources, and the rapidly growing demand
for energy to fuel economic growth and development, the energy sector is expected to continue to play
a key role in the future development of the economy. The GOI's main objectives for the sector are:
(a)  the economic (effciency) objective: to meet the country's energy requirements in a least
cost way, and conserve exportable petroleum reserves, principally by encouraging the
domestic market to substitute oil consumption by alternative fuels for which there is a non-
exportable surplus (e.a., natural gas and coal) or that are non-tradeable (e.g., hydropower
and geothermal).
(b)  the resource mobiiAion (fiscal and finandal) objective: to maximize the country's
foreign exchange earnings and budgetary revenues from the sector, mainly through the
export of tradeable energy resources, including oil, gas and coal, and to enable producers
of energy to recover their costs and obtain sufficient resources to finance their growth and
development.
(c) the social (equality and fairness) objective: to promote a regionally balanced development
of the country and to enable the majority of the people to afford the basic services provided
by electricity (lighting, cooking, transportation).
(d) the environmental objective: to promote the production and utilization of energy resources
in a manner that will ensure sustainable development and help conserve the environment for
future generationss.
1.20       Key elements of the GOI's strategy for the pursuit of the above objectives may be
summarized as follows:



-6-
(a)  peftreum produc prces: In setting its pricing policies, the GOT is guided by the principle
of maintaining the average price of petroleum products at international parity, (efficiency
pricing) while cross-subsidizing the price of kerosen with an implicit tax on gasoline
through the operation of the BBM (petroleum products) Fund (para. 1.5).
(b) electricity tariff: In pursuit of its social objectives, the GOI maintains electricity rates at
low levels for small residential, and small commercial and industrial consumers, who
account for about forty percent of PLN's sales. Periodic adjustnents in the average sale
price (the latest adjustment of 13 percent, effective February 1, 1993) have generally
maintained the financial performance of PLN at satisfactory level and the average price close
to the long-run marginal cost of supply (paras 3.7 and 6.17).
(c)  private sector partidpadon: While the public sector is expected to continue to play a
leading role in the supply of gas and electricity, there is scope for increased private
investment to take advantage of the additional resources and implementation capacity. As
initial steps towards enhancing private participation in the power sector, the Govermnent has
eannarked specific power generation projects totalling about 6,000 MW of capacity for
private investment on build, own and operate (BOO) basis (para. 2.11).
(d)  protectng the poor: To enable the majority of the people to afford the basic services
provided by energy (lighting, cooking, public transport), the GOI subsidizes the price of
kerosene, and the consumption of electricity by small residential, as well as small non-
residential customers. Although there are more effective ways to assist the poor, the social
and political implications of a change in price subsidies have not permitted rapid progress
towards their elimination.
(e)  regionaly balanced devdopment: To promote a regionally balanced development of the
county, the GOI has (i) maintained a uniform price strcture for petroleum products and
electricity in all parts of the country, and (ii) extended the supply of petroleum products and
electricity in a balanced manner to all parts of the country. These policies entail the cross-
subsidization of the higher cost of energy supplies to remote areas through net revenues
earned in the major urban areas.
(f) environmental protection: The GOI has put in place an institutional framework for the
management of the enviromental impacts of energy production. In 1992, the Government
created a Bureau of Enviroment in the Minstry of Mines and Energy with permanent staff
to supervise various environmental aspects in the energy and mining sectors. In addition,
a separate body in the Ministry of Mines and Energy, the Commission for Environment has
the authority to review and approve Enviromental Impact Assessments for all investment
projects.



-7 -
Insitutions In the Euerg Sector
1.21       The principal agency responsible for implemening Government policies in the energy sector
is te Miistry of Mines and Energy (MME). MME coordinates all activities in the energy sector and
supervises the state enterprises in the sector: Pertamina (oil, gas and geothermal), P.T. Bukit Asam
(coal), PON (gas distribution) and PLN (dectricity). Other ministries and agencies are also involved in
the sector, for example, the Ministry of Public Works is responsible for hydropower resource surveys
and the operation of multipurpose hydro plans and the National Atomic Energy Commission is
responsible for nuclear development. An inter-ministerial National Energy Board (BAKOREN)
coordinates energy policies and development with those of other sectors. BAKOREN is supported by a
Technical Committee (PTE) consistng of senior officials of different departments, chaired by the Director
General of Electricity and Energy Development (DGEED). The organizadon chart of the MME is shown
in Annex 1.1.

2
ELECTRIC POWER
The Electricity Subsector
2.1       The Electricity Act (Law No. 15 of 1985) defines the legal framework for
electricity subsector. The subsector comprises: (i) PLN, the State Electricity Corporation;
(ii) captive plants installed by private parties for their own use; (iii) three Goverment-sponsored
rural electric cooperatives; and (iv) a large number of infomal microterprises providing
electricity to clusters of customers in rual villages not served by PLN. The provisions of the
Electricity Act are amplified in Government Regulation No. 17/1990 for PLN and No. 10/1989
for others. The regulation of the subsector is the responsibility of the Minister of Mines and
Energy, who is assisted by the Director General of Electricity and Energy Development
(DGEED). DGEED is the chairman of the Supervisory Board which oversees PLN's operations
and reviews PLN's investment plans, budgets and tariffs. The Electricity Act also permits
establishment of private power producers, distributors and licensees. Presidental Decree No.
37/1992 issued in June 1992, clarifies the role of private power generation, transmission and
distribution of electricity for public and private use.
The Mlarket
2.2       During the 1970s and 1980s, the power sector of Indonesia has grown rapidly. The
installed capacity of PLN, grew from 0.5 GW in 1970 to 2.5 GW in 1980 and 9.2 GW in 1992,
as its sales grew from 1.6 TWh in 1970/71 to 6.5 TWh in 1980/81 and 31.5 TWh in 1991/92.
This achievement is impressive, particularly considering the expanse and archipelagic nature of
the country. Even so, the proportion of electrified households in Indonesia of 31 percent, is low
by regional standards. PLN is currently able to serve only about 48 percent of urban households
and 25 percent of rural households, whereas it is estimated that at least 90 percent of urban
households and two thirds of those in rmual areas can afford to purchase electricity. Annex 2.1
shows the growth in PLN's consumers and sales.
2.3       In spite of its rapid development, PLN accounts for just over half of the electricity
used by industries with the rest being provided by captive generation facilities operated by
industrial plants. The rapid emergence of captive generation in the 1970s and its continued
growth in the 1980s, bridged the shortfall in electricity supply from PLN, although at a cost, as
grid supplied elecricity is generally more economical than captive generation. Total installed
capacity of captive generation is about 5,900 MW, of which about 1,900 MW are connected to
PLN and operate on a standby basis, and 3,000 MW operate in isolation. Annex 2.2 shows the
growth in captive plants in Indonesia. In addition to industrial captive generation, the shortage



of electricity and absence of supply facilities has also given rise to thousands of village-level
microenterprises operating very small generator sets and providing a precarious service of
generally low standard to small cfAsters of rural customers.
The Demand for Electricity
2.4       The potential for growth in electricity demand in the future remains high. Even
though PLN's sales grew by 15 percent during the 1980s, there is still a backlog of unmet
demand at efficient prices, that has been supported by exanding the use of captive generation
and the proliferation of informal microenterprises in rural areas.  Further, the overall
electrification ratio remains low at 32 percent (para. 2.2), and the per capita electricity
consumption estimated to be about 270 kWh per year in 1991/92 is low compared with other
developing countries with similar or lower income levels. As a practical matter, not only has
PLN been forced to abandon for the time being the objective of takeover of existing captive
generation that is un-economic compared to grid supply, it has been signalling to potential new
industial customers seeking grid supply that such comnections may be slow in forthcoming.
Thus, the amount of captive generation capacity in industry is expected to increase well beyond
current levels.
2.5       It is estimated that the electricity demand will contine to grow rapidly, due to the
rapid economic growth led by export-oriented industries. The expectations of GDP growth range
from 6 to 8 percent per annum for the current decade, with the industrial GDP growing at 10
to 14 percent per annmn. PLN has prepared three sets of sales projections for its Java-Bali
system where the proposed project is located, based on low, medium and high growth scenarios,
with the following assumptions:
(a)  Low Growth. This was the scenario on which REPELfIA V (1989/90 - 1993/94)
was based. It assumed the average GDP growth rate of 5.4 percent per annum and
the growth in the industrial GDP at 7.0 percent per annum.
(b)  Medum Growth. This scenario assumes an average GDP growth of 7.2 percent
per annum until 2003/04, with industrial GDP growth assumed at 11.6 percent per
annum. The target ratio for the electrification of the households is 75 percent and
PLN's share of industrial demand is assumed to increase from about 50 percent at
present, and reach and stabilize at 68 percent.
(c)  High Growth. This scenario assumes an average GDP growth of 7.8 percent per
annun until 2003/04, with industrial GDP assumed to average 12.2 percent per
amnm. It targets the ratio for the electrification of households at 98.7 percent and
PLN's share of industrial demand is assumed to reach 97.4 percent.
The projections of sales for the Java-Bali interconnected system under these scenarios are shown
in Table 2.1.
2.6       The low growth scenario is considered too conservative as both the overall and
industi GDP growths since 1988 have consistendy surpassed the corresponding assumptions
underlying this scenario. The cur  discussion is centered around the selection of a projection
from among the medium and the high scenarios, to facilitate the planning of the generating
capacity additions for REPEITA VI (1993/94 to 1998/99). The projected growth in sales in

updated projections of economic growth, availability of fi'jance and implementation capacity.
The proposed project is justified under all the demand scenarios and is a part of the core
investment program of PLN.
Generating Capacity
2.8       The expansion plan for Java-Bali would be based on the use of hydro, geothermal
and gas-fired generating capacity to the maximum extent feasible. Current indications are that
about 1,200 MW of hydro, 220 MW of geothermal and 5,200 MW of combined cycle gas-fired
generating capacity could be added during 1991-2003 on the basis of known prospects to date.
The balance of the capacity of about 9,000 MW would need to come from coal-fired generating
plants. The Government has earmarked 6,400 MW of this latter capacity to be built and
operated by the private sector. The composition of the generating capacity in the Java-Bali

in Annex 2.3).

for transmission and distribution facilities are estimated. Since the development of the
transmission and distribution network is closely linked with the spatal distribution of demand,
precise plans for them can only be fonmulated 3-4 years ahead of the need. They are subject to
detailed annual reviews.
2.11       The issues associated wih the power investment plan are of two types. One, the
determination of the appropriate size of the investment plan consistent with the country's overall
economic development plans, resource availability and implementation capacity. This issue will
be resolved in the context of the formulation of REPELITA VI, the five-year national
development plan for 1994/95 - 1998/99. The Bank presented a report to the Government
(Indonesia: A Strategy for Infrasucture Development, Report No. 9672-IND of June 1991) that
deals with the issues and options confronting the Goverment. The second set of issues pertaim
to the optimm strategy for funding the investment program. In addition to PLN, the
Govermment intends to seek a more active role from private investors. For this purpose it has

projects is estimated to be over US$5.0 billion. The Bank is closely associated with the
Government's effort to expand private participation in the power subsector. It is providing
technical assistance to the Private Power Team in the Ministry of Mines and Energy, under the
Infrastructure TA Loan (3385-IND), for the ongoing negotiations of the first 1,200 MW coal-
fred power plant at Paiton in East lava on a BOO basis. To foster private sector participation
on a large scale in grid based supply using efficient technology, there is need to design an
appropriate institutional and regulatory framework. Towards this end, a study on the
institutional framework and regulation of the power sector in Indonesia is currently being carried
out by the Bank using Norwegian trust funds (para. 4.8).

2.13      The strategy for financing PLN's investment program has been a subject of
intensive dialogue between GOI and the Bank. The Bank has advocated the following principles
to the Government:
(a)  With a combination of measur, including efficiency improvement and tariff
adjustments, PLN should maintain a self-financing ratio above 25-30 percent.
Periodic tariff adjustments will be needed to permit this.
(b) The Government's contribution to PLN's investment requirements should be
gradually reduced and ultimately limited to that required for supporting socially
directed programs such as rural electrification. Also, the financial losses incurred
by PLN due to the Government policy of uniform tariff throughout the country and
life line rates for small residential and industrial consumers, should be compensated
on the basis of a tasparent mechanism.



- 13 -
(c) An optimal capital structure for PLN should be determined with an appropriate mix
of debt and equity (paras. 6.12-6.13). PLN should borrow in local and
international markets, and pay full cost of borrowing.
2.14      The Government is receptive to the above principles and expects to pursue it
through the discussion of PLN's five-year corporate plan with the Bank (pam. 6.22). Also, a
study for periodic formula-based tariff adjustment is being finalized. The study proposes
formula-based tariff adjustments to compensate for the variations in the cost of fuel and inflation
(para. 6.8), by providing for efficiency improvements in the base tariff.
2.15      The Government has requested a detailed study on the various issues involved in
the long-term strategy for financing of the power sector investment program, including the role
of the private sector. The proposed loan provides fuids for technical assistance to the
Government to carry out this study (paras. 5.24 and 6.22).
2.16      To ensure contanued soundness of the development program, the Bank agreed with
the GOI and PLN under the Suralaya Thermal Power Project (Loan 3501-IND), that: (a) each
year, PLN and GO will review with the Bank PLN's investment program so that the Bank can
exress its views on the priorities and the balance among generation, transnmssion and
distribution investments; and (b) the GOI will ensure that PLN has access to sufficientfunds to
finance its capital expenditures as reviewed and agreed with the Bank. These covenants are
currently being observed and were also agreed under the proposed project.
Demand Management, Conservation, and Efficiency Improvement
2.17      Supply Effiiency. PLN and GOI are conscious of the need for enhancing
efficiency in electricity supply as well as in end-use. On the supply-side PLN's efficiency along
many dimensions is generally in line with the better managed utilities among its regional peer
group. Coal-fired plants in Java, that account for about a third of all grid-based generation
operate at world class standards in terms of plant performance parameters such as fuel
consumption, and availability. A time series analysis of PLN's performance reveals a consistent
trend of efficiency improvements as well. Transmission and distribution (T&D) losses have
come down from a high of about 21 percent of net generation in 1983/84 to 14.1 percent in
1991/92. Looking to the future, PLN has targeted further efficiency improvements in operations
by reducing T&D losses on its Java-Bali grid system, which at present accounts for about 80
percent of all PLN generation, to 10.5 percent by the end of this decade. This efficiency
improvement would result in incremental energy savings of about 3,067 GWh in 2000/01, and
is equivalent to about a 500 MW savings in generating capacity. PLN is also undertaking a
program to increase the efficiency of its diesel plant operations, to reduce oil consumption and
increase plant availability.
2.18      Demand M              PLN is addressing efficiency improvement in end-use,
both indirectly through pricing, and direcdy through technological and behavioral interventions
in the manner in which electricity is used at present. The electricity tariff structure is designed
to reflect the long-runm marginal cost of supply, especially for large industrial and conunercial
consumer categories. The energy charges are suitably differentiated during peak and off-peak
times for these consumer segments. PLN's five-year corporate plan (para. 6.22), also identifies
explicitly target levels for savings from demand management, of about 317 GWh by 1996/97,
using a variety of direct measures (para 2.19). Institutionally, PLN has constituted a



- 14-
Conservation Steering Committee to coordinate, plan, and implement targeted efficiency
improvements on the supply- and demand-side. In particular, the Demand Managenent group
within this committee is responsible for designing, pilot testing, and encouraging implementation
of full-scale demand management programs in support of the demand management targets in the
corporate plan. To gear up for the task ahead, four individuals (three from PLN and one from
DGEED), undertook in July 1992 a two-week study tour of the US to learn first hand about
demand-side management practices and experience of the utility industry. Ongoing technical
assistance to PLN provided under a recent Asian Development Bank Loan (Power XXI Project,
May 1991), is facilitating the collection of data on customer load profiles and on electricity end-
use characteristics; data that constitute some of the building blocks for designing and developing
demand management programs.
2.19      PLN's draft corporate plan and strategy for meeting projected consumer demand
for 1993194-1996/97 identifies the following preliminary targets for demand management in
GWh:

PLN expects to capture thest savings praily from lighting efficiency improvement programs
targeted to each sector. The share of electncity use for lighting vanes by consumer group and
location. A recently completed study on demand-side management in Indonesia, funded by the
US Agency for International Development (USAID), estimates these shares to be 7 percent for
industry, and between 20 to 25 percent for commercial buildings, 53 percent for urban
households and 60 percent for rural households. The study reports that the replacement of
commonly used incandescent bulbs by compact fluorescent lamps (CFLs) can save about 75
percent of energy consumed for lighting. Although the initial cost of a CPL is significantly
higher than that of an incacent bulb, these high efficiency lamps are cost effective provided
the lamps perform to specifications under field conditions.
2.20      PLN has designed two pilot projects to field-test the technological and other key
aspects of program delivery. One pilot project, already approved by PLN's Board and for which
consultant selection is underway, is to be field-tested in the Jakarta area during 1993/94. The
second pilot project, for which PLN has undertaken a preliminary design, focuses on demand



- 15-
management in rural areas. This project has three program components: (i) a residential program
involving the use of CFLs to reduce peak load, (ii) a public buildings program that focuses on
the use high efficiency lighting alternatives and better electronic ballasts, and (iii) a comnercial
buildings programn that has two sub-components. One sub-component that centers around the
introduction of CFLs, and a second one that involves curtailing large cental air conditioning
loads in i_ntances where the consumer has a backup generation facility. The Bank is also
providing technical assistance to PLN in reviewing the building design of PLN's new
headquarters project from the perspective of energy efficiency, and to evaluate and recommend
specific opportunities to incorporate and thereby "showcase" building demand management
measures including building envelope improvements that result in inore efficient utilization of
electricity, high efficiency lighting and air conditioning systems, and opportumities for solar
thermal for hot water and solar photovoltaic systems for parking area lighting. Discussions are
also underway with USAI) to coordinate and prepare a demand management component to be
included in a future Bank loan, to implement the action plan for demand management in
Indonesia developed in the USAID study.
