3.2         Project Perfornance Audit Reports (PPARs) and Project Completion Reports
(PCRs) on the first 14 projects have been issued. The PPARs have concluded that the projects



- 17-
reviewed so far achieved the objectives set out at the dme of appraisal. Specifically, these
projects and the concomitant technical assistae programs, enhanced PLN's institutional
capabilities in many areas of utilities operation and management, particularly in the area of
project implementation, and helped PLN meet crical demands for electricity (para. 3.6). The
last PPAR (No. 7902 of June 26, 1989) concludes that the Bank has made a positive contribution
towards the developmen of the Indonesian power sector over the past 20 years. It attributes this
to the facts that: (a) there was a long-term vision for the development of the subsector and this
vision was shared by the Bank, Government and PLN; (b) the vision was translated into a long-
term strategy which was resolutely pursued, while remainmng flexible in the designing and
implementation of individual measures that fitted into this stegy and complemented each
other; and (c) a reasonable balance was struck between the physical and the institutional
development components of the projects. These conclusions drawn from the experience in
Indonesia are consistent with the Bankwide experience reviewed in OED's report of 1990,
entitled "Issues in World Bank Lending Over Two Decades." This report evaluated the outcome
of 180 Bank-financed power projects from 1974 to 1988 and suggested that sustainability of these
projects was highly influenced by institutional and country economic conditions as well as by
technical and policy factors.
3.3       In broad terms, the Bank's involvement with the power subsector in Indonesia can
be distinguished in four phases. In the initial phase (1968 until the mid-1970s), the Bank
concentrated on creating a national entity, PLN, to deal with sectoral problems and helped put
in place procedures and systems to manage and improve its initially small-scale operations. In
the second phase, (from 1975 to 1985), the primary thrust of the Bank's work was to assist in
a rapid expansion of the electricity supply infrastructure. In the third phase, the focus was on
strengthening the financial position and efficiency of PLN. In the current phase, the focus has
shifted towards the restructuring of the sector's regulatory framework and PLN's organization,
and increased private sector participation. Specific aspects of the Bank's involvement and
experience in the Indonesian power subsector are described below.
3.4       Initial Phase. In the late 1960s, when the Bank became involved in the power
subsector in Indonesia, PLN was in an exceedingly poor organizational and financial state. The
first power project (FY70) helped at once to identify PLN's weaknesses, as well as approaches
to overcoming them. Under a second operation in 1972, a long-term power development study
for Java was carried out that defined the objectives and conponents of the power system
development on which to base the Bank's involvement in most of the work under the second
phase. Another result of the first phase of the Bank's involvement was the promulgation of
Government Regulation No. 18 of 1972, that provided PLN with a higher, albeit still limited,
degree of autonomy, including responsibility for procurement and construction of most of its
facilities. This first phase also resulted in a new accounting system and a comprehensive training
program.
3.5       Capacity Expansion. During the second phase (from 1975 until 1985) of capacity
expansion, the Bank approved about one project every year. The infrastrucure components of
this series of projects helped PLN expand its generation capacity of thermal, coal-fired, hydro-
electric and geothermal power plants, and transmission and distributionfacilities. These projects
also supported the Government's objective of diversifying domestic energy consumption away
from oil by the use of coal, hydropower and geothermal. At the beginning of this decade,
PLN's installed capacity had grown to more than 10 times what it was at the begining of the
1970s, and to more than 2.5 times what it was at the beginning of the 1980s; Bank-financed



- 18 -
projects h; I made substantial contribudons to adding 3,800 MW of the approximately 6,400
MW of ge erating capacity operating in Java in 1991.
3.6       Institutional Development. The first major effort at institutional development at
PLN took place with Bank assistance in the mid-1970s, through the implementation of a
comprehensive Financial Recovery Plan (FRP) which took into account all aspects of PLN's
administration and operations. As part of the PRP and sibsequently through a long series of
loans, technical assistance was provided to improve PLN's skills in diverse areas including
system planning, demand forecasting, system operation, design of generation and transmission
facilides, operation and mainenance of power plants, project management, personnel
management, management information system and accounting. As a consequence, PLN has
made significant progress in improving the efficiency of its operations (Annex 4.2). In
particular, its transmission and distribution losses decreased from 20.8 percent in 1983/84 to
15.6 percent in 1990/91. The operation of the 1,600 MW coal-fired thermal power plant at
Suralaya in West Java (funded by four Bank loans), accounts for about 40 percent of the
electricity supplied by PLN in Java. The station achieved an availability factor of 93 percent
in 1991/92, which is comparable to the best in developed countries. Overall, PLN's capacity
for implementation, as well as for operation and maintenance, has grown substantially during
its association with the Bank. It now carries out on average an investment program of over $1
billion per year and connects over one million new consumers anmnally. The rapid expansion
has strained PLN's ability to improve its quality of service in terms of interruptions and voltage
stability. The Bank's dialogue on institutional development is now focused on providing PLN
with a stable financial environment, greater delegation and decentralization among its various
regions, stronger strategic planning, clearly defined accountability, and improvement in
distribution management and transmission reliability (paras. 4.4-4.8).
3.7        FInandal Perfonnance. The objective of the Financial Recovery Plan for PLN
to achieve the fill operating cost recovery was met in 1975/76. Thereafter, a self-financing ratio
(SFR) covenant was adopted in 1978 under the Seventh Power Project (Loan 1513-IND). The
agreed SFR target was 30 percent for 1985/86 with indicative targets for intervening years.
While the indicative targets for the self-financing ratio were met, the rate of return on assets
remained negative. Therefore, a rate of return (ROR) covenant was adopted under the
Fourteenth Power Project (Loan 2443-IND) in 1984. The ROR covenant was modified in 1987
under the Power Transmission and Distribution Project (Loan 2778-IND), to provide for separate
fiunacial performance targets for PLN's operations in Java (ROR of no less than 8 percent) and
outside-Java (financial breakeven situation), in view of their different operational characteristics.
Projections showed that PLN would be able to achieve a self-financing level of 25-30 percent
with these covenants. However, the government did not permit the corresponding tariff
adjustment in 1987/89. Consequently, although project preparation continued, the Bank did not
approve further loans in 1987 and 1988, pending the Government's required action on tariff.
The Govermient increased tariffs by about 25 percent effective April 1, 1989 and again by 20%
from August 1, 1991. As a result, PLN's financial performance has been satisfactory.
Electricity prices have been raised further by an average of 13 percent, effective February 1,
1993. However, for PLN to achieve the financial performance targets agreed under the
proposed project (paras. 6.5 and 6.8), further adjustment of the tariff would be needed in
1994/95 (para. 6.10). Also, in order to institutionalize the adjustment of tariff and provide
financial stability to PLN's operations, the emphasis of the Bank's dialogue on electricity pricing
has been shifted towards designing a mechanism thw would permit periodic and formla-based



- 19-
adjustment of electricity price to take account of variations in fuel prices, and inflation (para.
2.14 and 6.9).
3.8        Fuel Price Subsidies. The Bank has had a continuous dialogue with GOI on
petroleum products prices. Until 1979, the aggregate level of domestic fuel oil prices was about
one third of the international level. In 1980, in the context of the Ninth Power Project (Loan
1872-IND), the GOI agreed in principle to the elimination of subsidies, which were implemented
during 1980-84. The average price for a basket of petroleum products was raised to an
interational price equivalent, and has been maintained roughly at international prices by
subsequent adjustments (para. 1.5).
3.9       Implementation. Problems with the implementation of power projects have been
caused by delays in three major areas:  (i) selection and appointment of consultants,
(ii) procurement of equipment and goods, and (iii) acquisition of land and the right-of-way for
the construction of transmission and distribution facilities. At times, rigid budgetary procedures,
scarcity of local funds, unsatisfactory performance of local contractors, and shortage of trained
personnel within PLN, have delayed the implementation of previous projects. These problems
are not specific to the power subsector but are germane to the implementation of projects in most
sectors in Indonesia. In response to this experience, the Government has become conscious of
the need for improvement and has been taking corrective steps, such as dismantling the
organization for centralized control on procurement in 1988. However, approval of major
contracts is still centralized through the Coordinating Minister for Economy, Finance and
Development Supervision. Also, PLN is implementing cLanges in its internal procurement
procedures to optimize the procurement cycle for the award of contracts for goods and
equipment, and appointment of consultants, based on a strategy agreed under the Suralaya
Thermal Power Project (Loan 3501-IND signed August 26, 1992).
Bank Role and Strateg
3.10      The legal framework and regulatory process that has been in place in Indonesia
(para. 2.1), had been adequate to deal with the sector growth in the 1980s. However, some
sector issues are becoming increasingly important in the context of the rapidly growing size of
the electric power infrastructure needed to sustain the projected economic growth. While PLN's
implementation capacity, technical expertise and operating efficiency have grown significantly,
coping with the rapid growti in the infrastructure and providing reliable electricity supply
requires actions that include (a) institutional restructuring of PLN that involves decentralization
and corporatization of functions that have predominantly commercial objectives; (b) ensuring that
the development task is shared by the private sector so as to introduce greater efficiency through
competition and reduce the financial and managerial burden on the Government and PLN; and
(c) changing the regulatory framework to empower public and private institutions including
cooperatives in the sector to perform to their full potential.
3.11       Continuing improvement in PLN's efficiency in constructing, operating and
maintaining the electricity infrastructure still remains a major challenge since PLN will continue
to have a dominant role in the sector development for many more years. Considering that only
40 percent of the over 60,000 villages in the country have access to electricity, a large program
of development with social content remains an important part of the Government's strategy for
improving the welfare of the people. PLN is the only institution with the capability and
motivation for implementing the task. Similarly, the expansion of the generating capacity by



- 20 -
about 20,000 MW during the next 10 years is a large task that will need the technical and
funancial resources of both the private and the public sector. PLN's weaknesses are currently
perceived in its inability to meet all the economic demand in a timely manner and have service
reliability of a high standard. An appropriate institutional response to both these problems is a
reorganization of PLN that provides greater autonomiy to its regional units and leads to
progressive corporatization of the more efficiently organized units such as generadon,
transmission and distribution in Java. The required institutional measures were discussed in the
Bank's report on Power Sector Institudonal Development Review of December 1989. Under the
Suralaya Thermal Power Project (Loan 3501-IND) it was agreed that such reorganization of PLN
will be carried out and implemented over a three year period ending June 30, 1995. Action
plans for the implementation are currently being finalized and expert assistance to PLN is being
provided using funds from that loan (para. 4.7). Also, technical assistance is in progress to
strengthen PLN's corporate and financial planning and accounting functions; funded by the
Power Sector Efficiency Project (Loan 3097-IND). Various initiatives included in that project
have succeeded in improving several efficiency indicators of PLN, e.g., during 1986/87-1991/92
system transmission and distribution losses reduced from about 20 percent to about 14 percent,
capacity utilization improved from 55 percent to 67 percent, sales per employee increased from
287 MWh to 584 MWh, and overall consumers' accounts receivable reduced from 48 to 37 day's
sales (para. 4.4). Targets have been set for further improvemnent in these indicators for the next
five years in the PLN's corporate plan (para. 6.22). Further improvements in efficiency are
sought under this project (para. 4.5).
3.12      PLN is an autonomous corporation defined under the Electricity Act (1985) and the
Govemrnent Regulation (No. 17 of 1990). It is fully responsible for all aspects of the
construction operation and maintenance of its facilities (para 2.1). Its budget is approved by the
Government annually; the approval requires PLN to achieve a set of agreed objectives.
However, the Government exercises close control over several key areas of PLN's operations.
For example, electricity pricing has to be approved by the Government and there is no
mechanism for passing through variations in input costs such as those for fuel, in a timely
manner. Over the past few years PLN has been made free to select and recruit all its staff.
Although the staff salary and benefit structure has to be approved by the Minister of Mines and
Energy, the Governmuent has enabled PLN to increase its level of remuneration beyond that of
the Government employees to reduce differential from the market level and to enable it to attract
and retain qualified staff. The capital structure of PLN is closely controlled by the Government
who provides equity without any expectation of return on equity. However, there is currntly
emphasis on the financial performance and the Government has endeavored to make PLN
profitable and comply with the financial performance targets agreed with the Bank and other
institutional lenders. The Government guarantees PLN's borrowing but since 1992 the
Government has decided not to subsidize any new borrowings. PLN has also begun to raise
funds in the capital market through issuance of bonds. In order to introduce transaey in the
accunting of the cost for socially directed activities such as rural electrification that PLN is
required to pursue, an accounting system is being developed (paras. 4.12 and 4.14) to identify
the cost and subsidy required for such social activities. While privatization of PLN is not
currently on the agenda, steps for further conmnercialization are being pursued in the Bank's
dialogue with the Government on the above issues (paras 6.8 and 6.23).
3.13       Efficient pricing of electricity and fuels is an important objective that is pursued in
order to promote efficiency in consumption, conservation and resource mobilizadon. The Bank's
work in the sector is continuing to emphasize the importance of pricing from all these aspects.



- 21 -
Consequently, the prevailing (effective February 1, 1993) average selling price of electricity of
Rp. 150.5 per kWh (US cents 7.3) is close to the estimated average long-term marginal cost of
supply of about Rp. 168 per kWh (para 6.17). The average selling price does not include
connection fees paid by consumers. Maintaining the prices at the desired level has so far been
carried out by the Goverrnent through annual reviews and administrative approval. Currently,
the Bank is advocating the introduction of formula based tariff adjustments that would allow cost
increases due to changes in fuel price, and inflation to be automatically passed through during
the period between biannual reviews, while providing for efficiency improvements. A report
on this subject is currently under consideration by the Government (para. 6.9). On the front of
the pricing of primary fuels, the latest revision of the petroleum product prices in January 1993
has brought the price of all products except kerosene, at or above efficiency prices. Kerosene
price is still about 30 percent below the efficiency price, but the subsidy is being compensated
by the higher price of gasoline for automobile use (paras 1.5 and 3.8). The price of coal is
determined by the market. The price of the natural gas is above its economic costs for most
users, main exceptions being fertilizer and steel industries owned by the Government. The Bank
is prompting the Government to correct this situation as well. A recently completed study by
the Bank (Natural Gas Development Planning Study of July 1992) was discussed with the
Government; it is planned to follow it up by a "Gas Development Project" that will address the
pricing and the institutional issues (pam. 1.09).
3.14       Promoting the involvement of the private sector in the electricity sector is another
main objective that is being pursued by the Bank- in its dialogue with the GOI. Currendy, the
private sector provides a variety of services to PLN such as construcon of its facilities, serving
as contractors for a number of operations and maintenance activities, and providing services such
as meter reading, bill collection and distribution lines maintenance. The Government has also
earmarked a number of generating projects, totalling about 6,000 MW capacity, for the private
sector investment, on Build, Own and Operate (BOO) basis. The Bank is providing technical
assistance to the "Private Power Team" of the Ministry of Mines & Energy to negotiate the first
project at Paiton in East Java consisting of the construction of 2 x 600 MW coal-fired thermal
units (para. 2.11). The Bank has also promoted the concept of greater involvement of the
private sector in the rural electrification program, through cooperatives and organizations of the
beneficiary communities. The ongoing Rural Electrification Project (Loan 3180-IND) includes
components to reduce cost of rural electrification, promote day time and productive use of
electricity, and the development and training of personnel from rural cooperatives to take up a
larger role in the program. A second Rural Electrification Project, currently under preparation
will build on the experience gained from the ongoing project (para. 1.18). Other areas of private
sector involvement are cogenerationby industries, purchase of electricity from captive generators
licensing private generation in industrial estates and franchising geographical areas to licencees
to generate and/or distribute electricity. Also, the Govermment is encouraging private sector
participation by facilitating the sale of power to the grid from unsolicited power projects, as well
as the wheeling of such power supplies through the grid to third parties. In order to accelerate
these developments, the Bank is assisting in the reform of the regulatory framework in the sector
(para. 3.15).
3.15       On the regulatory side, a special Presidential Decree (No. 37/1992) was issued in
June 1992, to clarify the role of the private sector in the electric power sector. In addition,
some changes in the existing regulations are needed to promote greater efficiency in the sector.
The Bank is currently carrying out a study of the organizational, institutional and regulatory
framework in the sector. The study is funded by a grant from the Government of Norway and



-22 -
will discuss options for reform in the structure, institutional set-up and regulation of the sector.
The report is expected to be discussed with the Government in May/June 1993. The proposed
project provides for funds for technical aistance to Government to fiuther define and implement
the changes in regulations that are agreed (paras. 4.8 and 5.23).
3.16      The Bank along with USAID and ADB, has also increased its focus on the demand
side management and conservation activities (paras 2.18-2.20). The Bank's role in the proposed
project and in future projects will be to provide the Government with policy analysis and
technical assistance for the introduction of the required changes, while still supporting actions
to improve PLN's efficiency and implement economic investment projects to build the required
infrasructure.



-23 -
4
THE BENEFICRY
4.1       The proposed loan would be made to the Government of Indonesia (GOI). The
proceeds of the proposed loan, except for a part for technical assistance to GOI for carrying out
policy support studies to be managed by DGEED, would be onlent to PLN (para. 5.29), which
is responsible for the implementation of the proposed project. PLN, the sole govermment-owned
national power utility, has been the beneficiary of Bank Group lending to the power sector in
Indonesia since 1969 (para. 3.1).
PLN's Organization and  Ignent
4.2        PLN was established as a public corporation (Perum) under Government Regulation
No. 18 of 1972, with responsibility for the generation, transmission and distribution of
electricity, and the planning, construction and operation of electricity supply facilities. That
governing Regulation No. 18 was amended by Govermment Regulation No. 54 of 1981, and
superseded by Government Regulation No. 17 of 1990. PLN is managed by a Board of
Directors headed by a President Director, who is appointed by the President and is accountable
to the Ministry of Mines and Energy. The President Director has authority for all operations
of PLN. The Board currently includes five other directors with functional responsibility,
respectively, for planning, construction, operations, finance and administration. Operational
responsibility devolves upon 18 regions, and responsibility for major construction upon 13
project managers. Also reporting to the Board are several staff units responsible for power
research, education and training, management services, an internal audit unit called the
Corporation Inspectorate, the Java-Bali system operation and control unit, and an Engineering
Services Center (PPE). PLN's organization chart is shown in Annex 4.1. PLN's management
consists of dedicated professional staff of proven competence. Most of the managerial staff have
many years of experience in various facets of the company's operations. The staff morale is
generaUy high and the turnover low.
Institutional Development
4.3       PLN has grown rapidly in the past decade (para. 2.2). Since 1977/78, it has
connected new consumers at an increasing rate, from about 370,000 in 1978/79 to about 933,000
in 1991192. The rapid expansion of supply facilities, which is expected to continue over the next
several years, involves increasingly complex technical and managerial tasks, for which PLN will
need to continue to strengthen its insutional capacity. Areas that need strengthening have been
recognized, and actions and programs are either already underway or planned, in many cases
with assistance from the Bank. These include corporate and financial planning, general



- 24 -
accounting, inventory management, distribution management, consumer administration,
transmission reliability and manpower developmen.
4.4       In the area of operations, the main emphasis rests on improving the planning,
operation, maitenance and management of distrbution facilities. Under the Power Sector
Efficiency Project (Loans 3097-IND), PLN agreed to carry out an action plan for efficiency
improvements, which is under implementation. The major components of this action plan are:
(i) efficiency improvement in distribution including improvement in reliability; (ii) improving
corporate and financial planning and accounting; (iii) improvements in the operation of major
thermal power plants; (iv) improvements in the operation and maintenance of diesel power
plants; and (v) renovation of small hydro plants.  The inefficiencies in the distribution
management are considered to be one of the major problems facing PLN. These are manifested
in the form of high losses, low reliability of supply, long waiting lists and inadequate
information systems. PLN's distribution management functions are being strengthened through
technical assistance being provided under the Power Sector Efficiency Project (Loan 3097-IND).
The consultants have prepared and presented to PLN a number of programs that need to be
implemented over the next few years. These include: (i) changes in the organization dealing
with distribution at the district level, manpower training and matpower development; (ii)
development of information system and mapping; (iii) improvement in planning, construction,
materials management and design standards; (iv) changes in consumer administration, meter
reading and billing procedures; (v) improving cosmmications and maintenance facilities and
reporting systems; and (vi) loss reduction. Actions have been initiated on some of these
activides. Technical assistance providedunder the Rural ElectrificationProject (Loan 318-IND)
will help improve the planning of rural electrification subsector development. Also, PLN's
corporate planning and financial planning functions are being developed further with technical
assistance provided under the Power Sector Efficiency Project (Loan 3097-IND). Annex 4.2
shows some of the performance indicators for PLN for the years 1980/81 to 1991/92; they
indicate significant achievements in several areas. Specifically, manpower productivity improved
from a sale of 223 MWh per employee in 1983/84 to 584 MWh per employee in 1991/92, partly
due to subcontracting to private contractors for services such as meter reading and bill collection
(pam. 3.14); operating ratio improved from 102 to 84, and transmission and distribution losses
reduced from 20.8 percent to 14.1 percent, in the same period.
4.5       The areas that need closer attention of PLN are now (a) the efficiency of the
distribution system, to improve service reliability; and (b) the inadequacy of the transmission and
distribution network that has resulted in long lists of consumers waiting for supply. In order for
PLN to achieve quantitative ard monitorable improvements in these areas, agreement was
reace  ring the negotiations of the proposed loan, that PLN shall: (a) by October 31, 1993,
formulate and submit to the Bank, for its review and comments, proposals and action plans for:
(t) creating a databose for service reiabily and consumer waidng lists in its operations; and
(ii) improving servce reliabiltty and reducing consumer waiting lists in its operations; and
(b) tiereafter, implement the said action plans taing into account the commnus, if any, by the
Bank, in a manner satisfactory to the Bank.
4.6       To provide a broader framework and longer-term perspectve for the institutional
development of the power sector, the Bank carried out a Power Sector Institutional Development
Review (Report No. 7929-IND, dated December 20, 1989). The objective of the review was
to identify the regulatory and institutional conditions under which the continued rapid expansion
of the sector could be carried out with the greatest efficiency and effectivenss. The results of



- 25 -
the review suggest that the principal condition is the creation of an environment within which
PLN and the other participants in the sector will be motivated to apply their initiative and
creativity to reduce costs and improve sevice in the course of achieving the targeted expansion.
As a general direction, the creation of such an enviroment will require a shift towards greater
reliance on guidance through incentives rather than administrative intervention and on
decetraizd decision-making rather than centalized control, while also taking advantage of the
pressures created through competitive comparison. Based on the r eons of the study,
several issues relating to PLN's finances are being pursued through the proposed project and the
Bank's ongoing dialogue with the GOI. These issues include (a) electricity tariff, including its
periodic adjustment (paras. 2.14 and 6.9); (b) financing of investment program (para. 2.15); and
bearing of fill cost of capital by PLN (para. 6.23). In addition, initiatives are ujderway to
define and implement changes in the ins"tional framework and reguation of the power sector,
and the corporate structure of PLN (paras. 4.7 and 4.8).
4.7       To follow-up on the ro nions of the Bank's Power Sector Institutional
Development Review (para. 4.6), PLN has conducted a study to define the organizational
changes necessary for managing its operations more efficiendy and develop a plan of action to
implement these changes. The main elements of the plan consist of (a) providing greater
autonomy to the regional units for distribution management; (b) strngthening regional units for
planning and consumer administidon, and improving cutomer relations and billing;
(c) modifying budgetary procedures to enable the budget to be a management tool;
(d) introducing the concept of cost per kWh sold as a primary measure of performance;
(e) combining generation and transmission fiunctions in Java under one manager; (f) providing
semi-autonomous status to the four Distribution Units in Java; (g) simplifying and delizing
procurement; and (h) imple     a manpower devepment program. The Bank has reviewed
PLN's draft proposals on the actions required to implement these initiatives. As agreed under
the Suralaya Thermal Power Project (Loan 3501-IND), detailed action plans for implementation
were submitted by PLN to the Bank, and have been reviewed and discussed in April 1993. The
implementtion will commence soon, and the program is expected to be implemented in about
three years. Highest priority is bemg given to actions (e) and (f), for their completion by
December 31, 1994. The Suralaya Thermal Power Project (Loan 3501-IND) provides for
technical assistance to PLN in implementing the proposed actions.
4.8       The institutional measures discussed above are a prerequisite first steps towards
achieving the objective of cm ralon and corporatization of PLN. Concurrently, the
need is also recognized for evolving an institutional suctre and regulatory framework in the
sector that will promote competition and greater private sector participation in the areas of
generation and distribution of electricity. Such a development is necesary to mobilize requisite
resources for the continued expansion and efficient operation of the sector. In order to achieve
these objectives, a study (tided "Institutional Framework and Regulation of the Power Sector in
Indonesia") is being carried out by the Bank with the assistance of consultants, using Norwegian
trust funds, to define an appropriate institutional structure including the role of PLN and its
institutional arrangements, and also the roles of the private sector and cooperatives. The study
would also recommend necessary changes in the regulatory framework to facilitate the
implementation of a long-term sector development strategy. During the negotiations of the
proposed loan, underst uings were readed with the Govenment and PLN that they would
jointly discuss with the Bank the report on Insttutond Framework and Regulation of the Power
Sector in Indonesia, and that GOI would provide to the Bank by October 31, 1993 its proposals
for basic eleme of sectorfrwneworko nd a planforfunher acton, for the Bank's review and



- 26 -
comments. The proposed project provides technical assistance to the Government for further
studies and implementation of the changes in the regulatory framework (para. 5.23).
Staff Development and Trng
4.9       As of March 31, 1992, PLN had53,882 employees, of whom42,194arepermanent
staff. PLN has no difficulty in recruiting staff educated at levels up to technical high school,
but faces constraints in recruiting those with higher academic qualifications. Less than 5 percent
of its staff have university degrees, which is low by utility standards. To address its training
needs, PLN has a separate training department that plays an important role in PLN's efforts to
upgrade the skills of its technical and administrative staff. These efforts have been largely
successful. In 1991/92, about 9,260 staff received training-about 9,105 received training
domestically and about 155 abroad.
4.10      The Bank has supported PLN's training activities and human resources development
efforts, including the establishment of several training centers. Current efforts being supported
by the Bank include training in the areas of diesel/thermal plant operations, distribution
management, corporate and financial planning, general accounting and cash management, as well
as specific training activities in the rural electrification area and in public utility practices.
Accountin and Budgetin
4.11      PLN's accounting system, installed in the early 1970s, follows generally accepted
accounting principles, and normal utility practices, but it needs upgrading to keep pace with the
growing complexity and volume of accounting transactions. There is need for improvement with
respect to the completeness and accuracy of fixed asset accounting, depreciation, inventory
accounting and accounts receivables. The accounting system needs to produce more accurate
information in a timely manner for decision-malcing purposes. Also, PLN's internal procedures
for preparing budgets and monitoring actual expenditres need to be strengthened through better
coordination among and participation by the various functional directorates and regional offices
in the process, to enhance the budget's value as a management tool.
4.12      A major constraint to PLN's accounting has been a lack of trained senior
accountants and uniform application of accounting procedures. Since 1987, PLN has been
implementing an action plan to improve its accouning practices and functions by (i) establishing
a uniform accounting system, (ii) improving related support systems, (iii) sensitizing concerned
nonfinancial managers and staff to the importance of timely and accurate accountmng,
(iv) upgrading the skills of the accounting staff through in-house and external training courses,
and (v) computerizing the accounting process. PLN is also attempting to improve coordination
between its departments and its regional offices in the areas of budget preparation, and
monitoring and evaluation of performance. Technical assistance is provided under the Power
Sector Efficiency Project (Loan 3097-IND) to improve the accounting system. The Rural
Electrification Project (Loan 3180-IND) provides technical assistance to continue to enhance the
system for the segregation of PLN's accounting of its RE operations.
Billing and Coietfions
4.13      PLN's bills are computerized and prepared monthly at the Jakarta headquarters.
Overall collection performance is generally satisfactory. The receivables position of general



- 27-
consumer accounts has been good, as it has been kept below one and one-half months of the
yearly billing. The overdues from government users (including the Armed Forces and local
governments), which account for about 10 percent of PLN's total sales, have also been
maintained to under two months' billing.
Audit and Inernal Control
4.14       PLN's accouns are required by its charter to be audited by Govenment auditors.
The audits are carried out following geneci1ll accepted accounting practices and standards. The
audit reports in recent years had received qualif?Wd opinions from the auditors, most recently
with respect to the inventory accounts. PLN has made substantial improvements in the areas
commented upon by the auditors. In the reports for 1989/90 through 1991/92 there were no
qualifications. PLN makes special efforts to expedite the closing of accounts and has succeeded
in completing them usually within six months after the close of the fiscal year, as stipulted by
Government for all public corporations. While the unaudited accounts were generally finalized
and received in time by the Bank, there had been delays in the auditing of accounts. As a result
of joint efforts by PLN and its auditors, the time required for completing PLN's audits has been
reduced significantly. For 1991/92, the accounts were finalized in four months after the closing
of the fiscal year and the audited accounts were available within five months of the close of the
fiscal year. PLN's audited accounts are henceforth expected to be available within six months
of the close of fiscal year. During the negotiatons of the proposed loan, agreement was reached
that PLN would submit to the Bank its auditedfinancid statements and the report of such audits
no later than six months after the end of each fiscal year. In addition, PLN would furnish to
the Bank (i) a copy of its unaudited annual accounts whenever they are sent to the auditors; and
(ii) its segregated financial and operating information on rural activities within six months after
the end of each fiscal year.
4.15       PLN has an effective internal audit group which reports to the President Director
for various special investigations, in addition to keeping general and project accounting matters
under review.
ibsrace
4.16       PLN currentiy carries transit and marine insurance on equipment and materials in
transit. Projects under construction are covered by the contractor's all risk and erection
insurance. Fire and other hazards on most of the assets in operation are self-insured. In view
of the geographical spread of PLN's assets, any single loss would be relatively small by
comparison with total assets and operations, and this policy has been considered satisfactory by
the Bank. However, with some very large projects having been completed in recent years and
others now under implementation, there would be a greater concentration of assets in certain
locations than in the past. An understanding was reached under Loans 3097-IND and 3098-IND
that major works, plant and equipment at large power projects would be insured with outside
agencies. Accordingly, PLN is curently carrying commercial insurance for major operating
plants and equipment at several large power projects totalling about 4,900 MW, in and outside
Java. PLN is also considering proposals for creating a fimd for self-insurance of the remaining
assets.

5
THE PROJECT
Backrounld
5.1       The Cirata Hydroelectric Development is located in West Java on the Citarum
River, the third largest river in Java. The Bawk's involvement in hydroelectric development of
the Citarum River dates back to 1981, with the financing under the Sixth Power Project (Loan
1365-IND), of the feasibility studies for the Saguling and Cirata developments. That was
followed by the financing of the Saguling development under the Tenth Power Project (Loan
1950-IND) and Phase I of the Cirata development under the Thirteenth Power Project (Loan
2300-IND). In determining the installed capacity for Cirata, it was recognized that ultimately
it could provide economical peaking capacity to the grid, given its low incremental capacity cost,
and proximity to the main load center. It was concluded that, ultimately, 1000 MW of
generating capacity could be supported by available hydro energy. However, consistent with the
growth of base load thermal generation capacity, an initial capacity of 500 MW (4 x 125 MW
units) was selected, and substantial provisions were implemented in the initial project for future
addition of 500 MW. They included the construction of the underground powerhouse cavem
to accommodate the additional 4 x 125 MW units under Phase II of the project, which constitute
the investment component of the proposed project.
Rationale for Bank hvmolvement
5.2       To date, Bank involvement in the Citarum hydro development has encouraged
rational, responsible and environmentally safe and sustainable development of Indonesian hydro
resources. The proposed project is an appropriate vehicle for continuing the support of such
development, both through the Cirata extension, upgrading of PLN's environmental monitoring
capabilities particularly in relation to reservoirs, and through hydro inventory studies aimed at
identification of appropriate projects to enter the preparation pipeliie based on PLN's current
needs. In addition, institutional development objectives of the project are an integral part of the
Bank's broader strategy to develop PLN into a modern, commercially-oriented utility, that
reliably and efficiently meets the electricity needs of Indonesia.
Project Objectves
5.3       The objectives of the proposed project are to: (a) provide economical and
environmentally sound expamsion of PLN's peaking electricity generation capacity in Java and
improve service reliability; (b) update hydro inventories and carry out pre-feasibility studies for



- 29 -
promising hydroelectric schemes; (c) strengthen PLN's capability in the planning, investigation
and operation of hydroelectric schemes and in environmental management of power projects; (d)
study options for enhancing the environmental conditions inthe area of PLN's existing reservoirs
on the Citarun River. The institutional and sectoral objectives include: (a) strengthening of
PLN's financial position; (b) enhancing the dialogue on introducing a mechanism for formula-
based adjustment of electricity price; and (c) continuing the process of institutional restructuring
of PLN to provide for greater decentralizon, delegation and commercialization.
Projec Descipdon
5.4        The first stage (Phase I) of the Cirata project was constructed, with Bank assistance
(Loan 2300-IND), over the period 1983-88. It consists of a 125 m high rockfill dam and 3.9
million n' reservoir on the Citarun River, with intake structures and power tunnels delivering
water, at a head of A12.5 m to four 125 MW generating units installed in an underground
powerhouse, before discharging back into the river. The proposed project would include:
(a) implementation of Phase II of the Cirata hydroelectric development consisting of:
(i) construction of two 690 m x 8.4 ni diameter headrace tunnels, two surge
shafts 80 m x 18.6 m diameter, four steel lined penstock tunnels 200 m
x 5.2 m diameter, and four tailrace tunnels 138 m x 6.4 m diameter, with
associated outlet structures;
(ii) installation, within the existing cavern of the powerhouse of four
additional units each of 125 MW, including turbines, generators,
governors, regulator and control equipment; and
(iii) installation of transformers and switchgear for connection to the grid.
(b)  consulting services for the engineerng and construction supervision of the Cirata
Hydroelectric Phase II development;
(c)  studies to upgrade hydrological and environmental data bases, and improve data
collection practices;
(d)  studies to update PLN's hydroelectric inventories, and determine pre-feasibility of
selected schemes;
(e)  studies to identify options to enhance environmental conditions in the existing PLN
reservoirs on the Citarum River, and training of PLN staff in enviromnental
management (in relation to both hydro and thermal power projects);
(f)  training in dam safety and insumentation monitoring;
(g)  power sector institutional and regulatory framework studies for GOI;



- 30 -
(h) studies aimed at assisting GOI to determine a long-term strategy for financing
power sector investments; and
(i)  preconstruction stage engineering services for the Besai hydroelectric project in
South Sumatra.
Status of Devdopment of the Citun River
5.5        There are three major reservoirs with associated hydroelectric powerhouses on the
Citarun River; namely, Saguling, Cirata and Jadluhur. Jatiluhur, the furthest downstream was
developed in 1964 primarily for irrigation. Its 150 MW powerhouse provides about 820 GWh
of electricity per year to the PLN system. Saguling (700 MW, 2156 GWh) and Cirata Phase
1 (500 MW, 1424 GWh) constructed by PLN with Bank assistance, were completed in 1985 and
1988 respectively. Planning studies leading to the development plan for the Citarm river basin
are summarized in Annex 5.1. Salient features of the Cirata Phase II development are included
in Annex 5.2. No additional energy would be generated as a result of the 500 MW addition to
Cirata. Instead, the available energy would be concentrated into four hours daily in the peak
load period. This method of operation is described in more detail in para 7.4 and Annex 7.1.
Status of Preparation of Cirata Hydroelectric Phase II Project
5.6        Provisions in the Cirata Phase I project for future extension included (i) intake
srres, complete with gates and stoplogs serviced by an existing gantry crane; (ii)
underground powerhouse cavern, to accommodate the four units of Phase U, and (iii) short
lengths of power tunnels adjacent to intake structures and powerhouse. Furthermore, in
preparation for the project, PLN awarded contracts for fabrication of turbine spiral cases for the
Phase II units, and this work is now complete. PLN has also awarded a contract for the only
access road required (to the ventilation adit), and for base camp extensions. All land acquisition
for access roads, location of transmission towers, and switchyard extension is complete.
5.7        Final designs and bid documents, largely based on those for Phase 1, have been
prepared by PLN with assistance from foreign consultants. Prequalification of civil works
contractors has been completed and bids will be invited shortly. To date, PLN has been assisted
by consultants under a pre-construction engineering contract financed by PLN. Consultants have
been selected for the constucdon supervision phase of the proposed project.
5.8        Considerable geotechnical investigations were conducted in support of the Stage II
design process. These included 56 diamond drill holes with a total length of 3430 m.
Information from this program was supplemented by a complete review of rock conditions
encountered during the Phase I tunnelling. These investigations have led to an unusually high
degree of certainty of the geological conditions to be expected in the tunnels.
De A
5.9        Geology. Headrace tunnels will be driven through alternating beds of tuff breccia
and sandstone. The tuff breccia is intact and massive while the sandstone is fresh and well
layered. Favorable tunneling conditions are expected, with temporary support limited to
shotcrete and bolting. Por the large diamete surge shafts, rock conditions are expected to be
similar both to the headrame tunnel and the Phase I surge shafts. Temporary support as used for



- 31 -
Phase I, consisting of prestressed anchors, shotcrete and rock bolts, has therefore been assumed.
Favorable rock conditions in similar strata are also expected in the penstock and tailrace tunnels.
Again, support would be limited to shotcrete and bolting except near the tailrace portals, where
consolidation work and heavier support are expected. Geological conditions at the portals appear
to be considerably better than those encountered in the outlet area of Phase I.
5.10       Consucatn Materials. The existig andesite quarry will provide favorable
aggregates meeting the specifications. No significant problems are expected to be encountered.
5.11       Hydrology. No significant additional energy will be generated under Cirata Phase
H. With the additional capacity, energy generation will be largely concentrated in the four hour
peak period every day, thus enabling better utilization of the base load capacity. Cirata energy
estimates, derived in the Saguling/Cirata feasibility studies, were based on a 62 year series of
discharge data, indicating a very high degree of reliability of estimates. During its first six full
years of operation, Sagu}ing generated an average annual energy of 2,147 GWh, in comparison
with feasibility study long-term estimates of 2,156 GWh, despite an abnormally dry year in
1991/92, apparently associated with the El Nino climatic disturbance. This provides further
confirmation of the reliability and probable conservatism of the hydrological estimates for the
basin as a whole.
5.12       Sedimeon. Saguling/Cirata feasibility study esfimates indicated an average rate
of catchment erosion in the Citarum catchment of 1.5 mm per year, with the catchment above
Saguling yielding 1.2 mm per year. It was firther estimated that about 95 percent of the
sediment originating above Saguling would be trapped in the Saguling reservoir. Based on these
sediment rnoffs, sediment aggradation in Cirata reservoir was projected to begin to impair
seonal regulation after about 50 years with significant enoachment on live storage after about
100 years. Live storage was projected to fill after a further 60 years, after which generation
would be on a run of river basis. Measurements of sediment deposition in Saguling over the 6
1/2 years since reservoir filling have indicated an average deposition of about 0.9 mm, in
comparison with the 1.14 mm expected, indicating that feasibility study estimates were
conservative. Initial deposition surveys for Cirata have indicated a similar trend. A very long
life for the Cirata reservoir is therefore projected.
Envhronental Aspects
5.13       The existing Cirata dam, the related reservoir and an underground powerhouse that
contains 4 x 125 MW generating units, were all constucted under the Cirata Hydroelectric Phase
I development financed under the Thirteenth Power Project (Loan 2300-IND). Since the Cirata
Hydroelectric Phase II Project represents an underground extension of an existing project,
environmental impacts are not significant. Permanent works above the surface are limited to
extension of existing tailrace outlet structures and switch yard. Some surface areas will be
required for contractors' use and access, but these are not expected to exceed 10 ha in total.
PLN already owns suitable land for this purpose, and no resettlement of families/households is
required. The power station draws water from an existing reservoir and discharges slightly
upstream of another existing reservoir. Therefore the effect of concentrating flows during the
day will have no significant effect on existing riverine and lacustrine environments.
Environmental management, therefore, is primarily during the construction period with
appropriate provisions included in the bid documents for such aspects as: erosion control for
access roads and temporary works, disposal of uumel excavation muck and other spoil in existing



-32 -
disposal areas, potable water supply, waste disposal, health and amenities in contractors'
temporary camps and facilities. A total of about 2 ha of land in several parcels, required for
switchyard extension and location of transmission towers, has been acquired. This is low
productivity land for which compensation has been provided to 17 owner families at a rate of
Rp 2,000 per square meter. None of the affected 17 families needed to be relocated, since the
land acquired from them did not constitute their place of residence or a major source of
livelihood. With the amount of compensation paid, several of such families have already
purchased more productive land of comparable size. The project was approved by the Central
Commission for Envirownent, Ministry of Mines and Energy on January 26, 1993, for
implementation. A technical assistance component for studies relating to the environmental
conditions and management of PLN's existing reservoirs is also included in the project (para
5.21).

feasibility studies of selected schemes; (d) studies to identify options to enhance environmental

studies for GOI; (g) studies aimed at assisting GOI to determine the optimal financing strategy
for power system expansion, and (h) pre-construction stage engineering services for the Besai

- 33 -
5.18      Data Base Upgrading. The overall objective of this study is to improve the quality
and availability of the data required for hydro inventory and hydro preparation studies through
data base upgrading and data collection improvements. The data base upgrading will consist of
the establishment of a computerized archiving data base of climatic, hydrologic, and
environmental data (for primary and secondary data collected by PLN) within the framework of
long term plans for national date base upgrading, and training of PLN staff in its operation and
maintenance. The data collection component will consist of a review of PLN procedures for the
collection of hydrologic, climatic and environmental data and of the design of the PLN
hydrologic network, leading to recommendations for improvement, and training of PLN staff in
data collection procedures. Terms of reference for this study are included in Annex 5.4.
5.19      Hydro Inventory and Prefeasibility Studies. PLN's hydro inventory was last
updated in 1983. Since that time there have been a number of developments including the
completion of 1 in 50,000 mapping for Sumatra, completion of a number of pre-feasibility and
feasibility studies, including those financed under Loan 2300-IND and upgrading of the hydro-
meteorological network financed under the same loan. There has also been a high rate of
demand growth and interconnection of isolated networks, with consideration also being given to
further large scale connections, such as a Java-Sumara interconnection being studied under the
Sualaya Thermal Power Project. In addition, with large scale additions of base load plant in
the Java-Bali system, and limited possibilities for future hydro development, pumped storage
developments offer potential for economic peaking power. In this context, the project will
include a re-assessment of hydro/pumped storage inventories, with the following objectives:
(a)  update hydroelectric and pumped storage inventories taking into account
developments since completion of previous studies;
(b)  formulation of a long term strategy for hydro-resource development consistent with
Government long term objectives in relation to Indonesia's energy resources, and
the relative economics of hydropower in comparison to fossil fuel based power
production;
(c)  identification of priority schemes for preparation, and carrying out of investigations
and studies up to prefeasibility level for selected schemes;
(d)  identification of suitable candidates for implementation through a shortcut design
and construct process following feasibility studies;
(e) strengthening of the hydro-planning group within PLN (Survey Division,
Directorate of Planning) so that they have the capability and resources to carry out
this type of work on a continuous basis;
(f)  strengthening the capabilities of the national consulting industry in this field, so that
they are capable of supporting PLN in such studies in the long term.
5.20      Terms of reference for the inventory studies are included in Annex 5.5.
5.21      Envhronmental Studies. The expansion of the Cirata hydropower plant does not
pose any significant negative environmental impacts. Also, the sustainability of the existing or
proposed expansion is not affected by environmental factors. However, the Citarum River



- 34 -
reservoirs (Saguling, Cirata and Jatiluhur) are being polluted due to the discharge into the river
system of effluent from urban, industrial and agricultural activities. The pollution is not caused,
nor can it be controlled by PLN, and abatement measures are also outside its jurisdiction.
However, since PLN wishes to insure the long term sustainability of its reservoirs, it is in its
interest to obtain a better understanding of the sources of pollution, its effects on PLN
reservoirs, and appropriate methods of reservoir management to minimize its effects. Therefore,
under the proposed project, a study (with terms of reference in Annex 5.6) wiUl be undertaken
to:
(a)  review PLN's water quality monitoring program for the Cirata river, with the
objectives of obtaining a better understanding of the sources of major pollutants,
their effect on PLN reservoirs, and to provide calibration data for reservoir models;
(b)  develop and calibrate mathematical models of the PLN reservoirs, to help establish
biological assimilative capacity, enable prediction of the effectiveness of pollution
control measures, provide better predictions of stratification and seasonal de-
stratification, and enable definition of measures to minimize the effects of the latter
on fisheries;
(c) obtain a better definition of factors which lead to fish kills during reservoir de-
stratification, and to define mitigation measures that will minimize them;
(d)  identify the major sources of floating wastes, the removal and disposal of which
accounts for a large proportion of PLN's maintenance expenditures.
Representations would then be made to appropriate authorities to deal with the
problem at its source;
(e) study practices in rural/urban communities bordering the Saguling and Cirata
reservoirs which impact on reservoir management and water quality (e.g. sewage
and solid waste disposal, erosion protection and fertilizer use), with the aim of
identifying measures that could inexpensive!y reduce impacts, which could be
brought to the attention of the responsible authorities;
(    training of PLN staff in environmental management in relation to both hydroelectric
and thermal power plants.
5.22       Dam Safety and Instrumentation Monitoring. PLN has several dams which are
categorized as large dams under the ICOLD definition, including the 99 m high Saguling and
125 m high Cirata rockfill dams. Para 5.37 discusses the proposed strengteing of PLN dam
safety procedures.  Training of PLN staff in dam safety inspections and instmenation
monitoring, to enable them to fulfil their role in the upgraded procedures will be carried out
under the project. Terms of reference are included in Annex 5.7.
5.23       Insfitutional and Reguatory Framework Studies. A study is currently underway
(funded by a grant from the Governmmit of Norway, and managed by the Bank), to analyze the
current status the regulatory and institutional structure of the power sector in Indonesia, the
models and experience in other counties, and recommend an institutional and ownership
structure, and an appropriate regulatory framework of the power sector (pan. 4.8). The



- 35 -
proposed project provides for technical assistance to GOI in carrying out fiurher studies and
implement actions in this area.
5.24       FInabncng Strateg  Study. The investment requirements of the power sector are
large, and there is need to formulate a long-term comprehensive financing strategy (paa. 6.22).
The proposed project provides for technical assistance to GOI for a detailed study of this subject
for which draft terms of reference are included in Annex 5.8.

expected payments, there is no need for special account or statement of expenditure facilities.

with the standard disbursement profile for Indonesia power projects. The disbursements against
the loan wiU be somewhat faster than the standard profile, owing to the fact that the Cirata Phase

project. The project is expected to be completed by August 31, 1998, and the loan closing date

Operation
5.35      For the Saguling and Cirata dams financed by the Bank under the Tenth and
Thfirtnth Power Projects (Loans 1950-IND and 2300-IND) respectively, PLN has been carrying
out routine maintenance in accordance with progrms agreed by the Bank, together with regular
monitoring of dam instruntation, and head office review of monitoring reports. Also PLN's
Engieerng Services Center (PPE) has formed a group to carry out dam safety inspections of
all PLN's large dams. Dam safety procedures were discussed during appraisal. PLN has
followed procedures satisfctory to the Bank for the inspection of the Saguling and Cirata dams,
and has used a foreign consultant to review the inspection data. PLN has agreed that it would
have no objection to a procedure of having an international panel of experts to review dam
safety. Proposals are also under consideration by the Director General of Water Resources
Development (DGWRD), Ministry of Public Works to establish procedures and panels of experts
for dam safety monitoring for all major dams in the county. At one to two year intervals, PLN
would present a report on its inspections and monitoring in the intervening period, for review
by a panel of experts. The panel would inspect the larger dams and any others where PLN
monitoring had revealed problems. Agreements were reached during the negotiatons of the

FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
Past Opatg Result ad  nanel Posion
6.1          PLN's operting results and financial position for 1984/85 to 1991/92 are shown
in the financial statements in Annex 6.1 and are summarized in Table 6.1.
6.2          Prior to 1985/86, in the absence of ROR targets from the intemational lending
institutons, the Government's policy on electricity tariff permitted PLN's operations to function
at or near financial break-even. The Government provided substantial equity contribution
towards PLN's sizeable capital investment during this period. In 1986/87, PLN achieved a
positive rate of reurn (3.4 percent) on net revalued assets and its annu  self-financing rado
(SFR) was 23.0 percent. However, in 1987/88, PLN's financial performance deteriorated
substantally (ROR of -0.2 pecent and SFR of 7 percent). This result was due mainly to the
devaluation of the Indonesian currency in September 1986, price escalation, and substantial
increases in the proportion of oil-based generation in 19871/88 because of the drougt of 1987.
PLN's financial performance showed only marginal improvement in 1988/89. An average
ncrease of about 25 percent m the electricity rates approved by the Government, effective
April 1, 1989, and a subsequent increase in the rates by about 20 percent effective August 1,
1991, restored and improved PLN's profitability from 1989/90 through 1991/92.
6.3          PLN's oprating performance varies considerably at regional levels. PLN's Java
operations, which supply electicity through an n ed   systm, are largely commercially-
oriented and profitable. PLN's outside-Java operations, which are mainly small-scale and
dispersed over a large mnuber of islands, are mostly unprofiable, and have a negative impact
on PLN's overall finances. The selected 1990/91-1991/92 financial indicators for PLN's Java
and outside-Java operations are presented in Table 6.2.
6.4         In 1990191, PLN's Java operations generated an operating profit of Rp 562
billion, with an ROR of about 7.5 percent. The outside-Java operations, however, incurred an
operating loss of Rp 248 billion with an ROR of about -8.3 percen, and thereby reduced PLN's
overall operafing profit to Rp 314 billion, with an ROR of 3.0 percent. In 1991/92, the
operating loss incurred by the outside-Java operations increased to about Rp 273 billion, with
an ROR of -7.6 percent, whereas the Java operations produced an operating profit of Rp 934
billion with an ROR of 11.7 percent. PLN's fiial performance in 1990/91 was adversely
affected by domestic fuel price increases in May 1990 averagmg about 15 percent, which were
not passed through until August 1991, when tariff adjustments were made. The May

FIancal Objecives
6.5              Under the Suralaya Thermal Power Project (Loan 3501-IND signed August 26,
1992), PLN was required to: (a) realize for its Java operations, in 1991/92 and each fiscal year
thereafter, an annual ROR of not less than 8 percent on the average net revalued fixed assets in
operation in Java, and (b) for its outside-Java operations, achieve a break-even situation in each
of its fiscal years beginning with fsal year 1993/94, i.e., total revenues equivalent to no less
than the sum of their (i) total operating expenses, and (ii) the amount by which debt service
r uirements exceed the provision for depreciation.

above are that PLN should cover its operating expenses, realize positive annual rates of return,

effecdve August 1, 1991, and by an average of 13 percent effective February 1, 1993. The
Government also increased the petroleum products prices in January 1993, and eliminated
subsidy on diesel oil (pam. 1.5). The price for diesel oil was increased by about 27 percent and
that for fuel oil by about 9 percen. Of the tariff increase of 13 percent effective February 1,



-46 -
1993, 7 percenge points represent pass through of these increases in fuel prices. As most
electricity generation outside-lava is oil-based, the fuel price increases would impact more
severely on the outside-Java operations than the Java operations. PLN is expected to achieve
for its Java operations the ROR target of at least 8 percent in 1992/93 and 1993/94. For the
outside-Java operations the profitability would deteriorate in 1992/93 with operating loss
expected to reach about Rp 453 billion in that year, compared to an operating loss of Rp 273
billion in 1991/92 (parm. 6.4). With the February 1993 iease in electricity prices the outside-
Java operations would show subsial improvement with the operating ratio declining from an
estimated 140 percent in 1992/93 to 115 percent in 1993/94 but not enabling a financial break-
even in that year, as required under the revenue covenant for the outside-Java operations (para.
6.5). It is estimated that a tariff inse of about 17 percent effective April 1, 1993 (or a
correspondingly higher figure at a later date) will be needed for PLN to achieve the financial
break-even in 1993/94 at the prevain fuel prices. As the Govemment has only recently
approved a large electricity tariff increase which, in percentage terms, is nearly double that
required to pass through the recent fuel price increases, it has indicated its inability to further
raise electricity prices soon, because of the need to contain inflationary pressures, and in view
of social and political considerations. For the short term, i.e., for 1993/94, the Java operations
would be able to meet the 8 percent ROR target of the revenue covenant, but the outside-Java
operations would be unable to achieve the covenanted financial break-even. In view of the
above, it was agreed under the proposed project to retain the rewnue covenants agreed wider
the Suralaya thermal Power Project (Loan 3501-N)D), but sluft the target date from 1993/94
to 1994/95for the outside-Java operatons to acideve the break-even (para. 6.5). The rationale
of using the break-even revenue covenant to promote efficiency improvements in PLN's outside
Java operations needs to be revisited in view of the overriding impact of fuel price increases on
financial performance, and the continued rapid expansion of the infrastructure to meet the
projected demand growth. Moreover, since the formulation of the financial break-even covenant
in 1987, the characteistics and the size of PLN's operations has been changing, with some of
its regional operations outside Java becoming increasingly commercial in nature. Consequently,
there may be a need to review the revenue covenant and to prepare, if necessary, an alternative
covenant for the future. Among options that could be considered would be more conventional
measures of financial performance, such as annual RORs on PLN's average net revalued fixed
assets in operation in Indonesia as a whole and cash generation measured against capital
expenditure.
6.9         To ensure the continued achievement by PLN of its financial objectives.
discussions have been held with the Government and PLN for introduction of a mechanism for
periodic formula-based adjusutent of the electricity tariff to take into account, inter alia,
variations in fuel prices and inflation. Under the Suralaya Thermal Power Project (Loan 3501-
IND), agreemen was reached tbat, by Septmber 30, 1993, Government will fiurnish to the Bank
for its review and comments, proposals for a tariff adjustment mechanism and an action plan to
implement such mechanis-. The Bank has reviewed and commented on a draft report prepared
by PLN on a tariff adjus_tm   mechanism. During the negotiations of the proposed loan,
understandings were reached with the Government that by June 30, 1993, a revised draft report
would be submitted for revew by the PLN's Board of Supervsors and other governmental
agencies, and that the proposed taiff adjustment mchanism would be submitted to the Minister
of Mines and Energy, by August 31, 1993.  These would be followed by higher level
consulations to obtain governmental decision to permit the application of the mechanism,
staring in 1994/95.

6.10        The financial projections, which are based on compliance with the financial
break-even covenant in 1994/95 (paras. 6.5 and 6.8), show that PLN's overall profitability
would improve substatialy with its overall anmual rate of return rising from about 2.1 percent
estimated for 1992/93 to about 4.3 percent in 1993/94. With a tariff increase of about
20 percent, effective April 1, 1994, that is estimated to be required for PLN's outside-Java
operations to achieve financial break-even in 1994/95 (para. 6.8), the annual rates of return in
subsequent years would be around 9-10 percent and the annual tarff increases required would
be mainly to compensate for the variation in the input costs, e.g., fuel and inflation. The capital
structure would remain satisfactory; the debt as a percentage of debt plus equity (excluding
revaluation surplus) would rise from about 30 percent in 1991/92 to about 42 percent in
1994/95, and would thereafter remain in the 40-43 percent range during the projection period.
PLN's debt service coverage ratio would be in excess of 2.0 throughout the projection period.
An existing covenant under Loan 3501-lND requires that PLN may not incur any additional debt
unless a reasonableforecast of its net revenues after expenditures for each year during the term
of the debt to be incurred shall be at leastl.5 times its projected debtservce reqWirements. This
covenant was also agreed under the proposedproject. PLN's current ratio is expected to be in
the range of 1.2 to 1.4 through most of the projection period; this would provide adequate
liquidity.
6.11         The financial projections show that during the forecast period, PLN's operations
outside-Java would show a deterioration in 1992/93, with the operating ratio rising from
131 percent in 1991/92 to 140 percent in 1992/93; however, the operating ratio would improve
to 115 percent in 1993/94 and to 100 percent in 1994/95, the latter would result from an
electricity tariff increase of by about 20 percent (para. 6.10) to achieve the covenanted financial
break-even in that year (paras. 6.5 and 6.8). For PLN's Java operations in 1992/93, the
operating rato and the annual ROR will be 81 percent and 8.1 percent respectively and a similar
result is expected for 1993/94. With a 20 percent tariff increase in 1994/95, the operating ratio
would decline to 59 percent and the ROR would rise to 17.7 percent in that year. Thereafter,
while the operating ratio will remain around 65 percent, and the annual RORs would be about
13-16 percent. Consequently, during the forecast period the Java operations would exceed
substantially the minium ROR target of the revenue covenant (para. 6.5), the result of the
policy of mainining uniform tariff and the requirement to achieve the financial break-even for
PLN's outside-Java operations. This provides another reason for reviewing the revenue covenant
(para. 6.8).
6.12         In the financial projections, PLN's capital expenditures (including interest during
construction) projected for 1992/93-1999/00 are derived from the investment requirements shown
in Annexes 2.5 and 2.6, and total about Rp 67,300 billion ($32,830 million) in current prices;
they exclude the capital expenditures for generation projects proposed to be implemented by
private investors (para. 2.11). The Bank financial projections assume that for the proposed
investment program to be implemented by PLN, the financing strategy will follow the principles
discussed in para. 2.13. The financing plans assumed in the financial projections for the
REPELITA V (1989/90-1993/94) and REPELITA VI (1994/95-1998/99) are presented and
compared to that for the REPELHTA IV (1984/85-1988/89) in Table 6.4. Details are given in
Annex 6.1.

for the REPELITA IV period, with the annual contribution declining to about 39 percent in
