Performance Monitoring
6.14         For monitoring the financial performance under the revenue covenants discussed
in para. 6.5 for PLN's Java and outside-Java operations, PLN has been providing the Bank with
separate bistorical financial results (beginmng with its fiscal year 1986/87) and forecasts on a
proforma basis, but not separate audited financial statements. Accounting for PLN's Java and
outside-Java operations is separate except for overhead expenses, for which regional accounts
are not maitained.
Tarffs
6.15         PLN's tariff schedules, which became effective February 1, 1993, comprise 24
consumer categores. For medium and large industrial consumers, large conmnercial consumers,
large social institutions, and for large office buildings, time-of-day pricing is in effect. The
February 1993 and earlier tadff schedules are shown in Annex 6.3.
6.16         Under the Suralaya Thermal Power Project (Loan 3501-IND) it was covenanted
that (a) PLAV will periodically review its long-rn marginal cost (LRMC) and tariff structure, and
discuss the results of the review with the Government and the Banks and O) before December 31



- 49 -
in each year, PLN will review the adequacy of its tariffs to meet the requirements of the revenue
covenants (para. 6.5). Such a review will take into account the results of the review of the
LRMC-based structure. 7hese covenants were also agreed under the proposed project.
6.17         In June 1992, PLN completed a study of its LRMC of supply in Java, which was
reviewed by the Bank. The study indicated that PLN's average revenue in 1991/92 would have
been Rp 168/kWh, if the tariff of each consumer category was set at the estimated LRMC of
supply in Java. PLN's average revenue, based on the current tariffs (effective February 1,
1993), is about Rp 151/kWh, not including the connection fees paid by consumers. Therefore,
the level of the electricity tariff is close to the average LRMC of supply. However, cross-
subsidies exist between different consumer groups. At present, 99 percent of all residential
customers (93 percent of all PLN customers) that are on tariffs S-1, R-1 and R-2 are subsidized.
This group has an average monthly consumption of 61 kWh. Despite the subsidies, the
household electrification ratio in rural areas remains low at about 25 percent. The lump sum
payment required to pay for the initial installations and connection, as well as the fixed monthly
demand charge serve as barriers. There is clear evidence that the household electrification ratio
in rural areas can be increased significantly by rolling these charges into the energy charge.
Regarding the subsidies to small industries and businesses, there are far more direct and efficient
means to stimulate enterprise creation and expansion, since the cost of electricity is well under
five percent of total production costs in most instances and it is simply not an influencing factor
in most business decisions regarding capacity utilization or expansion. Indeed evidence strongly
Lnudicates that most businesses are more concerned about the reliability of supply than its price,
and are willing to pay the price for reliable source. To compensate for subsidies to the small
consumers, electricity prices to the large industrial customers (tariffs 1-4, 1-5), the large
commercial customers (tariff U-3), the large hotels (tariff H-3), and the large residental segment
(tariff R-4) are therefore higher than the economic cost of supply. Further rationalization of the
structure of electricity prices is necessary, but it is being thwarted by strong social and political
seniment in favor of small consumers.
Taxes
6.18         In accordance with the prevailing tax legislation effective since 1984, PLN is
subject to a 10 percent value-added tax (VAT) and to corporate income tax. The Government
currently bears the VAT on the foreign contracts, and PLN bears it on the local contracts; this
policy is assumed to continue throughout the projection period. With respect to corporate
income tax, the cuurent Indonesian tax code permits the use of accelerated depreciation of assets
in operation, instead of the straight-line method used by PLN in its financial accounting. As a
result, PLN does not expect to pay any corporate income tax for its fiscal year 1991/92, and
during the projection period. In calculating the rate of return, however, any income tax
payments will be taken into account.
Overl Developmet Fund
6.19         Under Government Regulation No. 18 of 1972, that established PLN (para.
1.10), the provisions relatng to the disposal of PLN's annual net profit after corporate income
tax require that 55 percent of such profit be set aside for "Dana Pembangunan Semesta" (DPS),
or the Overall Development Fund for which the required payment should be made to the state
treasury. Regulation No. 18/1972 also provided that the Minister of Finance may directly
reinvest in PLN for the expansion of its facilities the DPS funds payable by PLN. A



- 50- 
Government Regulation No. 17 of 1990 has superseded PLN's govermng Regulation No. 18 of
1972, and the earlier provision that allowed the Minister of Finance to reinvest in PLN the
proceeds of the DPS funds payable by PLN is no longer applicable. During the negotiations of
the loan for the Power Transmission Project in March 1991, the Bank noted that PLN had a
sizeable investment program that was expected to grow substantially over the next several years
and expressed the concern that any large payments by PLN to DPS (as required under
Government Regulation No. 17 of 1990) would significantly reduce its self-financing capability.
Recognizing the need to ensure that PLN would have sufficient funds available to carry out its
investment program, the GOI indicated that its equity contributions in the past had accounted
for a large share of PLN's investments and that it would continue to make its best efforts to
provide funding to ensure that PLN would be able to implement its investment program. Under
the Suralaya Thermal Power Project (Loan 3501-IND), it was covenanted that GOI will take aU
such actions as shall be necessary to ensure that any transfer of profits from PLV to the DPS
shall not prevent PLN from inplementing its agreed investment program (para. 2.16). This
covenant was also agreed under the proposed project. With respect to the about Rp 70 billion
payable by PLN to the DPS, for the period up to 1989/90 based on the provisions of
Government Regulation No. 18 of 1972, the Government has agreed for PLN to pay that amount
in ten equal ammal installments, the first of which was in 1991/92. For later years, the amounts
payable by PLN to the DPS would be spread out in five equal anmual installnents.
Thaning Policy
6.20        PLN formulates its long-term investment program spanuing a ten-year-horizon,
which is updated periodically. The plan is reviewed by the GOI and a five-year time-slice of
it is incorporated in the country's development plan, the latest of which (REPELITA V)
commenced on April 1, 1989. Based on the approved framework of the five-year plan, PLN
prepares an annual plan and a budget, which is approved by the Government. In recent years,
the Government has shown a strong commitment towards these plans and has taken steps to
enable PLN to implement them. During REPELITA IV, the Govermnent's contribution
amounted to about 40 percent of PLN's investment expenditures, whereas PLN's own
contribution was only 13.6 percent with the balance of about 46 percent being financed by
borrowings (paras. 6.12-6.13). The level of PLN's own contribution was low because it was
not permitted any upward adjustment in its tariff during the REPELITA IV period.
6.21        An existing covenant (Loan 3501-lND) relating to the long-term aspects of PLN's
financialplanning was also agreed under the proposedproject. This covenant requires that, as
part of its ongoing annual planning activities, PLN should prepare a ten-year financial forecast
based on its proposed development plan, and also prepare for the first five years of the forecast
period a financing plan that should indicate the amount of external financing and the appropnate
mix of equity and debt, and if possible the potential sources of financing. The covenant also
requires that PLN's development plan, and related financial forecast and financing plan will be
reviewed and adopted by its Board, and that these plans and the forecast will be submitted to and
discussed with the Government and the Bank. Technical assistance is being provided under the
Power Sector Efficiency Project (Loan 3097-IND) to help develop furher the financial planning
capability in PLN (para. 4.4).
6.22        The Government proposes to continue to finance PLN's investment from three
sources, namely, PLN's internal funds, borrowings and Govermment contributions. In view of
the large and rapidly growing investment requirements of the sector, there is a need to develop



-51 -
a strategy for the long-term financing requirements of PLN. Also, there is need to develop a
suitable framework that will allow PLN to reasonably formulate and implement investment plans
in a predictable flnancial environment. As a step towards developing such a framework, there
is a need to define the strategy for PLN's borrowings, including access to local capital markets.
During the negotiation in May 1992 of the loan for the Suralaya Theml Power Project (Loan
3501-IND) an understanding was reached that for PLN's Five-Year Corporate Plan
(RENSALITA) for 1992/93-1996/97 that was under preparation and expected to be finalized by
September 30, 1992, the various planning assumptions to be used for the preparation of the
RENSALITA were discussed and agreed to by the concerned Government agencies such as the
office of the Coordinating Minister for Economic and hdustrial Affairs and Development
Supervision (EKUIN & WASBANG), the Ministry of Finance and BAPPENAS so that PLN's
corporate plan is consistent with the macroeconomic prospects and resource availability. A GOI
interdepartmental group had been established for this purpose with the coordinating responsibility
vested in the Director General of Electricity and New Energy, Ministry of Mines and Energy.
In October 1992, PLN completed its draft five-year corporate plan for 1992/93-1996/97. The
plan, which was submitted to GOI, has been reviewed by the Bank. It outlines the key issues
that emerge from PLN's intemal and external enviromnent; analyzes different scenarios of its
development program and financing requirements; identifies its development program and
financing requirements; identifies its medium and long-term objectives; and lists key performance
targets and outlines action programs to achieve those targets. The overall investment
requirements (both of PLN and the private sector) of the sector for 1992/93-1998/99 are
estimated to be in excess of $25 billion equivalent in constant 1991 prices (paras 2.11 - 2.13).
The Bank has communicated to GOI its comments that there is need to decide on the demand
growth scenario, the role of private power, electricity pricing, demand side management
measures, and a clear strategy for financing the large investment requirements of the power
sector, keeping in view the availability and expected growth of captive generation (paras 2.3 and
2.4). The Government, has now decided to formulate a long-term national electrification plan
that would form the basis of a detailed electricity development plan that would be prepared for
inclusion in REPELITA VI (1994/95-1998/99). For this purpose, an interdepartmental
committee headed by DGEED, would consider various issues including those raised by the Bank.
These exercises are expected to be completed by January 1994. The provisions in REPELITA
VI would provide the framework for revising PLN's RENSALJTA. During the negotiations of
the proposed loan, understdings were reaced with the Govermnent tha it will (r) keep the
Bank informed of the progress in theformuAidon of a long-term national electrification plan and
the preparation of a detailed electricity development plan for inclusion in the sixth Five-Year
Development Plan (REPELITA) and revision of PLN's five-year corporate plan (RENUSALTA),
and (ii) provide draftproposals to the Bankforits review and comments. The proposedproject
provides for such technical assistance to GOI for a detailed study of the vanous issues involved
in the long-term strategy for financing the overall investment requirements of the power sector,
for which draft terms of reference have been prepared (para. 5.24).
6.23         PLN is authorized by its charter to borrow from local and foreign lenders and
to issue its own obligations. Until recently, PLN's local borrowings had been only from the
Government-owned Banks. By Government Regulation No. 60/192 dated September 19, 1992,
PLN has been authorized to issue bonds in stages up to a maxiwum amount totalling Rp. 1.2
tr; on, with the approval of the Minister of Finance. In September 1992, PLN issued the first
annual tranche of such bonds amounting to Rp. 300 billion, which was oversubscribed. It is
proposed to raise a further Rp. 900 billion through bond issunce in 1993/94, and the practice
is expected to continue in later years. To support the bond issue, PLN has strengthened its



- 52 -
treasury functions and reduced the time required to finalize the annual financial accounts and
complete their audit (para. 4.11). In line with its policy for all State Corporations, the
Government secures all foreign borrowings on behalf of PLN and bears the foreign exchange
risk. Until the Power Transmission Project (Loan 2778-IND) approved in January 1987, the
onlending rate included a banking fee of only 0.25 percent over the Bank's standard variable
interest rate to cover the admistratve charges. In line with the Bank's objective of gradually
adjusting the onlending rate to a level comparable to one that would apply if the loan was made
directdy to PLN, fees were increased to 3.5 percent under the Power Transmission Project (Loan
3349-IND) approved in June 1991. Under the Suralaya Thermal Power Project (Loan
3501-IND) approved in June 1992, the Government agreed to onlend the proceeds of that Bank
loan to PLN for 20 years including a grace period of 5 years, at an interest rate equal to the sum
of (a) the Bank's standard variable interest rate, (b) a variable fee equal to the average of the
annual rate of the change in the value of the Rupiah against the US dollar, during the previous
three years, and (c) a service charge of 1.0 percent per annum. The onlending interest rate
would be applied to the principal amount outstanding under the related subsidiary loan, expressed
as the aggregate of the Rupiah equivalent, determined as of the respective dates of withdrawal,
of the value of the currency or currencies deemed to have been relent to PLN. This
arangement, which ensures that PLN compensates the Government for the foreign exchange risk
that will be borne by the Government, would also be agreed in respect of the onlending terms
of the proposed loan (pam. 5.29). Similar arrangements now apply to PLN's foreign borrowings
from multilateral agencies such as the Asian Development Bank, and increasingly to those from
the bilateral agencies as well.
Asset Revaluation
6.24         PLN's assets have been revalued periodically. The last detailed revaluation was
done in 1979 as required of all public corporations under instructions of the Ministry of Finance.
The revaluation was recorded in the books and was accepted by the Bank. Fixed assets acquired
after March 31, 1979 are appraised at cost. Following the currency devaluation in September
1986, the Govemment issued Regulation No. 45/1986 dated October 2, 1986, providing for the
revaluation of assets of businesses and public corporations, acquired through September 12,
1986. The Regulation specifies the annual revaluation factors for the years 1970 to 1986 for
adjusting the asset values as of January 1, 1987. For purposes of monitoring the revenue
covenants (para. 6.5) PLN has prepared a revaluation of its assets for 1979/80-1986/87 based
on the Regulation. The methodology used by PLN for asset revaluation is satisfactory to the
Bank. A covenant, under Loan 3501-AD, that requires PLN to revue annualy itsxfied assets
(including work in progress and conswners' contriuons) and accumulated depreciatonfor the
purposes of the rate of return and break-even calcuations under the reven  coven
(para. 6.5), was also agreed under the proposed project.

7
PROJECT JUSTIFICATION
Demand Growth and Need for the Project
7.1         Over the past decade, the sales of PLN in the Java Bali system, which accounts
for approximately 80 percent of PLN's total electricity sales, increased from 5.1 TWh to 22.4
TWh at an annual rate of 15.9 percent. A summary of the acual and projected annual growth
of electricity sales by sector for the period 1980-2000 is presented in Table 7.1 below.
Despite this substantial increase in supply, PLN has not been able to meet the overall electricity
demand, and captive generation facilities continued to grow at a sustained rate of about 9 percent
per annum.
Table 7.1: JAVA-BAu SYSgm
ACTUAL AND FORECASU ELECTrrY SALES OF PLN (1902000)
Actual Average           Forecast Average
Annual Growth (%)        Annual Growth (%)
1980-1990                1990-2000
Residential                               12.2                       10.9
Commercial                                10.1                       12.1
Industrial                                23.2                       13.3
Public                                     8.4                       10.8
Energy sold                               15.9                       12.3
7.2          Over the last year, the supply/demand imbalance has worsened.  Power
curtilment by rotation has been enforced in the Java Bali system for industrial users, thereby
limiting their operation to only five days a week, leading to considerable loss of production. In
addition, as at December 1991, PLN is carrying a backlog of new connections totalling an
estimated load of 3840 MW. The proposed project, as part of the least-cost generation plan for
the Java-Bali system, will assist in improving the balance between demand and supply.



- 54 -
Project as a Least Cost Generating Alternatve
7.3          PLN's least-cost generation plan for the Java-Bali system  includes the
commissioning of Cirata Phase 11 in 1997. The project was selected by PLN's dynamic
programmig based optimal generation expansion model, even after allowing for savings
expectd from energy conservation and demand side management measures (paras. 2.17-2.19).
Further, the project imposes insignificant enviromnental penalties (para. 5.13). Hence, the
project is an ideal candidate for system expansion even under integrated resource planning
metbodology.
7.4         In addition to the study by the least-cost generation model, a determnistic
analysis (prented in Annex 7.1) was carried out to compare the addidon of Cirata II to the
system in comparison with a thermal alternative. In accordance with this analysis the existing
500 MW at Cirata operates pardy on base load (95 MW for 24 hours) with the remainng 405
MW operating only dunng the four hour peak penod. With the addition of the 500 MW of
Cirata Phase II, the entire capacity will operate on peak. With this addition, therefore, there is
a net loss to the system of 95 MW of dependable base load capacity, in retrn for a net gain of
595 MW of dependable peaking capacity. The system expansion with Cirata Phase II plus a
dependable 95 MW of coal capacity is therefore compared with a dependable 595 MW of gas
turbine capacity. The discount rate at which the two alternatives yield the same PV (equalizing
discount rate) is 31.8 percent, indicating a very strong preference for the Cirata Phase II
alternative.
Project Size and iming
7.5         Insaled Capacty.  Studies carried out at feasibility stage convincingly
demonstrated that the low icremental capacity cost of Cirata justifies the maximm practicable
installed capacity. Given the shape of PLN's daily load curve, and operating consrain, a
minijmu capacity factor of 16.66 percent, corresponding to an average of four hours per day
was stipulated. This indicated an overall installed capacity of 980 MW, which was rounded up
to 1000 MW (i.e. operation at slightly less than four hours per day). The first stage installation
was 500 MW, and the remaining 500 MW will be provied under the project.
7.6         Tlming. PLN's least cost planning studies indicate that Cirata II should be
commissioned (as early as possible) in 1997. This is to be expected given the large blocks of
base load capacity which will come on line over the net five years, and since rtrements of
peaking plant with capacity approximately eqfal to that of Cirata Phase I are planned during this
period. Since the additonal pealing capacity can be completely absorbed by the system in its
instalation year, there is no need for staging the instalation.
Enomic Rate of Return
7.7          The project is a component of PLN's least cost expansion plan for the Java-Bali
interonneted system. Its benefits depend upon complementary investments in transmission and
distnrbution as well as coordinated operations within PLN's power system. Therefore, economic
viability has been established by calculafting an Iternal Economic Rate of Return (IERR), on the
basis of the incmental cost and benefit streams associated with the 1992/93-1997/98 time slice
of PLN's least cost expansion plan, of which the project is a part.



- 55 -
7.8          Capital costs considered for the economic analysis include investments in
genemion projects with commissioning dates duriag the selected time slice 1992193-1997198.
In addition, transmission and distribution investments over the same period are included. These
costs are exclusive of taxes and duties. Estimates of incremental fuel consumption are derived
from the results of an operation simulation model of the power system. Incremental fuel costs
were then estimated by applying fuel prices defined in Annex 7.2.
7.9          Benefits of the investment program have been estimated by the revenue from
electricity sales on the basis of the average electricity tariff, and supplemented in the case of
residential and industrial consumers by the consumer surplus.
7.10         In the case of industrial consumers, their willingness to pay is estimated by the
cost of self generation from captive diesel plants. These costs are estimated to be typically in
the range Rp 150/kWh to Rp 300/kWh, depending upon plant scale and utilization. The analysis
conservatively assumes an average willingness-pay of Rp 200/kWh, or about 1.4 times the
present average tariff of Rp 140/kWh (6.83 USM/kWh). This implies an average consumer
surplus of 2.93 USO/kWh.
7.11         For residential consumers, their consumer surplus is estimated by two
components: (a) consumer surplus in the diverted market i.e., cost savings resulting from the
substitution of kerosene by electicity for lighting, and 0b) the incremental consumer surplus in
the new market i.e., the benefit of increased consumption because of the availability of lower
cost electricity (Annex 7.3). With the average residential tariff of about Rp 147/kWh (7.17
USc/kWh), the components together are conservatively estimated to be 5.95 USC/kWh.
7.12         The internal economic rate of return (IERR) on PLN's investment program time
slice 1992/93-1997/98 works out to 19 percent under prevailing tariffs and taking into account
the consumer surplus for residential and industrial consumers as discussed in paras. 7.9-7.11.
The economic rate of return based solely on PLN's current tariff (i.e., excluding consumer
surplus) works out to 11 percent (Annex 7.4).
Senidtivity Analysis
7.13         The internal economic rate of return decreases to 17 percent if all investments
in generation, and transmission and distribution increase by 10 percent. With benefits calculated
solely as revenue at the current tariffs, the economic rate of return would reduce to 9 percent.
Benefis
7.14         The project would help PLN meet increases in peak demand for electricity in
Java and improve service reliability. The project would also help strengthen PLN's financial
position by enhancing the dialogue on the introduction of a mechanism for the periodic
adjustment of electricity tariffs, improve PLN's institutional capabilities, and achieve greater
efficiency in the power subsector by promotng reform in the institutional and regulatory
framework in the sector to empower public and private sector institutions in the sector to
perform to their full potential.



- 56 -
Project Risks
7.15         The risks generally associated with hydroelectric projects are related to potential
cost and schedule overruns due to geological and hydrological uncertainties, and contractor and
construction supervision inadequacies. For the current project, geological risks normally
associated with underground hydroelectric projects are reduced to a minimum, because of the
knowledge gained in the construction of Cirata Phase I development, extensive investigations of
the new tunnel alignments, and the fact that the power cavern is already complete. Hydrological
uncertainties are also at a very low level, since only a small amount of work is to be done in the
river downstream of the dam where flood risk is negligible. Care has been taken in the
prequalification process to ensure a fully qualified civil works contractor, and the full
involvement of experienced international consultants in the construction supervision process and
PLN's appointment of a project manager for the Cirata Phase II development would reduce the
project management risks to a minimum. As a result the risk of cost overruns and time delays
is small.
7.16         There is little risk that the proposed project would become redundant if the
projected level of electricity demand does not materialize, since the project is a part of the core
expansion program of PLN and is justified under all demand scenarios, i.e., low, medium and
high (para. 2.7). There is a risk that the planned large expansion program for the sector may
not be carried out for lack of sufficient financing and/or implementation capacity, which may
result in supply shortages and consequent disruptions in the national economy. This risk is
reduced through Bank-supported measures aiming at enhancing sector efficiency through changes
in the institutional and regulatory framework of the sector, as well as in the organizaional
structure of PLN, which would enable the public and the private sectors (including cooperatives)
to apply their full potential towards meeting the technical, managerial and financial requirements
of the sector.

-68-                        ANNEX 5.1
INDONESA
CIRATA HYDROELECTRIC PHASE II PROJECT
DEVELOPENT PLAN FOR THE CITARUM  IVER
1.          Water resources development in the Citamm River has proceeded in
accordance with an overall development plan, shown schematically in Figure 1 of this annex,
providing for the full development of the three major storage sites: Jatiluhur, Cirata and
Saguling. These storages provide a very high degree of regulation, enabling average annual
energy generation of 4400 GWh, irrigation of about 300,000 ha of land, and supply of about
34 m3/s of water for municipal uses in and around Jakarta.
2.           The optimization of the Cirata development was carried out as part of the
1981 feasibility study, using funds provided for this purpose under the tenth power project
(Loan 1950-IND), within the framework of the overall basin development plan. The
taliwater level of the Cirata power station was fixed considering: (a) the duration of high
water levels at Jatiluhur, and (b) the length of the waterway. Several alternatives were
considered to utilize the remain  head between the tailwater of Saguling (+252 m) and
Jatiluhur (full supply level + 111.6 m) of Jatiluhur. These included various full supply levels
for Cirata in the range of +205 m to +235 m, various minimum operating levels and a
possible run-of-river development between Cirata and Saguling at Rajanandala where the bed
level is 225 m. The optimum solution was found to be a Cirata full supply level of +220 m
and a minimum operating level of +205 m.
3.           The Department of Water Resources Development (DGWRD) and Jatiluhur
Dam Authority (POJ) concurred with PLN on the conclusions of the study, but noted that
some experts had raised the possibility of trans basin diversions from rivers in Western Java,
to supplement flows in the more arid regions of Central Java. This possibility could militate
in favor of a higher dam at Cirata. With this in mind the detailed engineering for the Cirata
dam was carried out considering the possibility of an eventual increase in full supply level
to the extent technicaUy feasible. During the subsequent investigation phase the project
board of consulas advised their opinion that geological features on both river banks limited
the maximum safe operating level to +235 m. Designs were therefore developed for an
in,tial full supply level of +220 m, with minimm provi lons included for a possible future
raising to +235 m. During the preparation of the current project DGWRD advised that
alternatives involving raising of the Cirata dam are low on their list of possibilities for future
supplemenation of water supply to Jakarta. Therefore no specific provisions are made in
the current design for future increases in reservoir level. Nevertheless, the possibility
remains open. It will be noted from Figure 1 that future pumped storage development has
been postulated at Cirata, in parallel with the existing power development. This addition is
not planed in the near future, because of the existence of more economically attative
pumped storage sites elsewhere in Java. No provisions are necessary in the current project
for fiture pumped storage additions.



-69-                        A5.1
4.           Reservoir operation studies carried out as pan of the Cirata optimization
considered the best method of operation of the three reservoirs. These studies confirmed the
intuitive conclusion that Jailuhur, the furthest downstream and largest reservoir should
continue to operated primarily to meet irrigation and water supply requirements. Saguling
should operate to maximize energy generation, and Cirata releases should be adjusted
according to the needs of irrigation and power. These operation studies confirmed that the
proposed storages could meet the overall multi-sectoral targets of the basin development plan
(pam 1). operations of the various reservoirs and co-ordination of the operating authorities
have proceeded smoothly, since the commissioning of the PLN reservoirs. The proposed
project will not change operations of Cirata, except on a daily basis. This will have no effect
on the multipurpose operations of the reservoir system.

-77-                           ANE  5.4
NDONESIA
CIRATA HYDROELECTRIC PHASE H PROJECT
TERmS OF REFERENCE FOR UPGRADE OF HYDROWGICAL DATA BASE
Bakgound
1.        The final report on the hydropower potential of Indonesia, dated October 1983,
made a number of recommeendations. These recommendations were designed to provide
Perusahaan Umun Listrik Negara (PLN) with a hydro-planning capability and to address
deficiencies in the hydrologic data and topographic mapping that were noted during the study.
Progress has been made in both of these areas but further work, particularly in the archiving of
hydrologic data, is now urgently required to enable the planned hydro inventory review to
progress. At the completion of the hydro-power potential study, the consultants passed over to
PLN a data base of the monthly rainfall and streamflow data collected for the study. This data
base was supplemented by a number of programs that used this monthly data to evaluate hydro-
power potential. Up to the corpletion of the inventory study in 1983, the software and data
base were stored on an IBM 370, at which time they were transferred to an IBM 4341, where
they remained until 1985 when this computer was transferred to Semarang. No data has been
added to the data base since then. Attempts to transfer the software and data to a nuinfram
computer in PLN Pusat have been unsuccessful.
2.         At present, hydrologic data is collected by PLN-LMK from the approximately 160
streamflow and 350 rainfall stations installed following the 1983 review. The recorders are
mainly weekly or monthly chart types with some long period chart recorders. PLN has begun
introducing some data loggers that will be unloaded by PC in the field. The stations are serviced
by local operators and the stations are visited and gauged by PLN-LMK staff from Jakarta
approximately once a year. This limits the number of gaugings, and hence the quality of the
rating curves, and a training program to teach local operators to carry out gaugings is in
progress.
3.         PLN-LMK has set up the data collection procedures, maintenance and provide
material needs for hydrological stations through the PLN regions. The collected data is sent to
PLN-LMK. PLN-LMK in Jakarta plot the gaugings, produce rating curves, check and process
the charts and then archive both the charts and the summarized data. For rainfall, hourly values
extracted by hand and stored on a PC as ASCII text files with one flle per station-year.
Individual station-years are identified by their eight character MS-DOS file names consisting of
an abbreviation of the station name and the year. Yearly rainfall data is published in monthly
summary format of the Agency for Research and Development of Public Works, the Research
Insttute for Water Resources Development, Bandung (RIWRD). For streamftow, average daily
values of stage height are extracted by hand and then converted to stream flow using a rating
table derived from the occasional gaugings. Streamflow data is not archived on the computer
and is only available in manuscript form. Because of the manual processing, re-rating after
control changes is not feasible, while missing record periods are not estimated and the digitizer
is not used.



-78-                          ANNEX 5.4
4.         PLN also have a mober of telemetered stations that transmit operational hydrologic
data by radio to the Public Works hydrology organiation (RMWRD) in Bandung. At present the
hourly data is stored on the telemetry master stations at the RIWRD with floppy disc backup.
The RIWRD are the main collector of hydrologic data in Indonesia and in the past they archived
PLN data. At present R1WRD hydrologic archiving system needs some improvement. The
Honeywell based system, funded some years ago, is no longer usable due to operation,
maintenance and software problems. As a temporary alternative the data is being stored using
the digitizing PC's. An APPLE n is used to digitize weekly pluviograph charts. However, this
software cannot handle long period chats and dtese are extracted by hand. Data is stored on
floppy discs and not backed up due to the large mnbers of floppies involved.
S.         Streamflow charts are digitized and archived using an IBM PC. At present the
Bandung office can only handle about 100 of the over 1300 stations each year. To improve this
situation 10 of the 27 regional offices have been provided with the software and hardware to
digitize streamflow charts. The RIWRD intend to have all the digitizing done by the regions
with only central archiving done in Bandung. The RIWRD have problems with unstable controls
and carry out 10 to 12 gaugings at their stream flow stations each year and use a manual rating
curve estimation procedure. Rating extrapolation is done using conventional techniques.
6.        In March 1992 the various parties involved in the collection of hydrologic data in
Indonesia held a symposium and agreed that:-
-    the long term objective is to have a common hydrologic data base for Indonesia;
-    the RIWRD Bandung should be the manager of this common data base.
Hence any decision on a hydrologic archiving system for PLN must also consider the RIWRP
in Bandung and work towards the agreed long term objectives.
OBJECTIVES
7.         The overall objective of this study is to improve the quality and availability of the
data required for hydro inventory studies by data base upgrading and data collection
improvement. Because of the need to upgrade the data base ahead of the hydro inventory studies
the data base improvement section should proceed expeditiously.

system.

agreed long term objectives of R1WRD and PLN.

Review of Current Data Bases and Archiving Systems
8.         The consultant will carry out a detailed review of the curent status of hydrologic,

Softwar
9.         When the planned RIWRD hydrologic archiving system is operational the data base
management will be transferred to the RIWRD and the PLN and RIWRD archives merged. The
chosen system must allow PLN to operate their section of the archive to meet the needs of hydro
nventory studies before and after this merging. The chosen systemn must be either a system used
by an operating authority or an adaptation of a system used by an operating authority.
Compatibility with other existing systems (e.g. the IBM 370 or the Inst. of Meteorology &
Geophysics) must also be considered.
10.        The consultant will make recommendations concerning the  provision of an
archiving system for hydrologic, climatic and environmental data that meets short term and long
term PLN objectives. The archiving system should include the following features:
(a)   Flexible Data Input. The system must accept data from a wide range of sources.
e.g. text files, digitizers, telemetry, data loggers, computers and manual entry.
(b)  Flexdble Data Output. The system nmst output raw and processed data as prinm-
outs, plots, floppy discs, tapes, etc. or as required by users.
(c)   User Interfce.  The user interface must be user friendly and suitable tor
occasional as well as skilled or routine users.
(d)  Multple User Aecess. Several users must be able to access the data base at one
time with little loss of system performance.
(e)   Archive security. The archive needs to be partitioned to limit edit access to some
sections to designated users only. While a system of user privileges is necessary
to limit user access to some options e.g. low, intermediate and high skill plus data
base manager.
(t) Back-up. The system must provide regular back up to ensure the safety of both
software and the data.



-80-                          ANNEX 5t4
(g)  Data storage. The system must be capable of storing all hydrologic, climatic and
envirownental data collected to date and all data that will be collected for the
foreseeable future. The system should store all raw data and some processed data.
A variety of time series data types are needed (e.g. spot samples, event totals,
continuous instantaneous and the average, maximum, minimum and total in
preceding interval.)
(h)  Customized Input/output. It should be possible for skilled users to alter output
formats, change units, use English or Indonesian text, combine files (e.g. flow x
concentration = mass rate), import and export spreadsheet and statistical package
data and generally devise new processing and output methods without the need for
computer programmers or software alterations.
(i)   Peripherals. The system should support digitizers, plotters, printers, graphics
terminals and links to PC's. The peripherals should be queued devices rather linked
to one work station to make them available to all users.
(j) Gauging and Ratings. The system should store and calculate gaugings and have
a data base of current meter ratings. Rating plots should label individual gaugings
and a range of rating estimation and validation tools are needed.
(k)  Manag_eent Tools. A range of data base management tools are required to keep
track of data input progress, rating development, percentage of missing or
estimated data and auditing of any editing of time series data by each user.

INDONESIA
CIRATA HYDROELECTRIC PHASE H PROJECT
HYDRO INVENTORY AND PREFEASIBILITY STUDIES
TERms OF REFERENCE
Background
1.        Perusahaan Umum Listrik (PLN) completed a Hydro Power Potentials Study in
1983. The study estimated an overall hydropower potential of ... MW of which ... MW was
considered to be economically viable. Out of a total of 1,275 schemes identified, 101 schemes
were evaluated as proniising schemes for development in the short to medium term taking into
account both the relative economics of the schemes, and the current and potential demand in the
systems served. Since that time there have been a number of developments including new I in
50,000 mapping for Sumatra, completion of a number of pre-feasibility and feasibility studies,
an additional period of hydrologic data collection and upgrading of hydro-meteorological
networks. There has also been a high rate of demand growth and interconnection of isolated
networks, with studies recently being completed for the full interconnection of Sumatra and
consideration also being given to further large scale connections, such as a Java-Sumatra. In
addition, with large scale additions of base load plant in the Java-Bali system, and limited
possibilities for future hydro development, pumped storage developments offer potential for
economic peaking power. In this context, PLN are commissioning a study to re-evaluate
hydro/pumped storage inventories.
Objecdves
(a)  update hydroelectric and pumped storage inventories taking into account
developments since completion of previous studies;
(b)  formulation of a long term strategy for hydro-resource development consistent with
Government long term objectives in relation to Indonesia's energy resources, and
the relative economics of hydropower in comparison to fossil fuel based power
production;
(c)  identification of priority schemes for preparation, and carrying out of preparation
works and studies up to prefeasibility level for selected schemes;
(d)  identification of suitable candidates for implementation through a shortcut design
and construct process following feasibility studies;
(e)  strengthening of the hydro-planning group within PLN  (Survey Division,
Directorate of Planning) so that they have the capability and resources to carry out
this type of work on a continuous basis;



-86-                           ANN  5.5
(f)   sr ig   e capabilities of the national consulting industry in this fteld, so that
they are capable of supporting PLN in such studies in the long term.
SCOPE OF WORK
Data Colection and Review
2.         Assembly and review of all existing relevant reports relating to: hydro and water
resource development; power system development; and energy resource development; power
system development; and energy resource development. Assembly of existing topographic,
meteorological, hydrologic, geologic, land use and cost data relevant to the study. In this
context it should be noted that PLN is in the process of upgrading its computerized data base's
of this includes a GIS, and setting up a cenhtalized hydrologic, climatic and environment data
base.
Inventory Update
3.        In principle, it is envisaged that the inventory update will cover at least the 360
schemes which passed the first screening of the 1983 study. However, this rule should be
applied flexibly particularly, where significant new basic data has become available in the
intervening period. In such cases it may be appropriate to re-evaluate schemes which did not
pass the first screening, or even to edevelop the inventory for areas where completely new
mapping is now available. Promising schemes which have been identified since the original
inventory should also be included. Prospective pumped storage developments with the potential
to serve the Java/Bali system should also be identified.
4.         The inventory update should be based on a computerized system, capable of
developing power and energy estimates, quantities and cost estimates from basic data. In
principle the system should have the capability to be upgraded by PLN to incorporate new cost
dat or improved functional relationships (e.g. turbine cost curves versus head, power output and
number of units). PLN have an exisdng program developed under the 1983 studies, which has
the advantage of staff familiarity, and existing data base. During proposal preparation, the
consultant is expected to review the existing program and data base, and tai  into account
technology developnt since the development of the program, recommend the course of action
to be taken with regard to program conversion/modification/updating, software
purchase/development and eqwpment reuiements. His technical and financial proposals should
be based on these recommendations. If proprietary software is proposed the proposal should
include provisions for maintaining and upgrading this software during the study period and at
least five years beyond it.
EnvfronUeNMulpose Scening
5.         As far as possible, each scheme in the inventory should be subjected to preliminary
screening to assess major   me l ips, resetlement implications, conflicts with other
potential users, and any favorable multipurpose impacts. This screening should be a pre-
requisite to selection of a scheme for the reduced inventory (par X).

Load Growth/System Develspuent Studies
6.         in parallel with te inventory studies, the consultant will develop load growth
projections in a form suitable for use in the study which are consistent with PLN overall load
growth projections. In large int  ed systems PLN aW lomerated data is likely to be
sufficient. In less developed regions, projections should to be developed by load ceer in the
detail necessary to formulate generation/transmission options for the region in question. Since
the primary purpose of load growth studies is to determine the speed at which hydro energy
could be absorbed, load growth projections should in princple be unconstrained i.e. not
determined by the ability of the system to meet demand.
7.         Costs of thermal generation alternatives, specific to the region in the question (unit
size, fuel source), should also be developed.
8.         Based on the above, and taking into account exisng commitments and short term
plans, and the lead times required for hydro project development the consultant shall cary out
generation/planning studies in sufficient detail to determine how hydro potential, together with
associated transmission can economically be incorporated into PLN system expansion plans.
9.         To assist PLN in long term strategic planning, the consultant should not constraint
the planning studies to individual regions, but should also consider inter-region and inter-island
transfers e.g. Sumatra to Java and possibly Kalimantan to Java. Both such transfer could also
be considered in the context of possible coal based energy transfers.
Selection of Reduced Inmveutory and Schems for Preparton
10.        Based on the above studies, the consultant should derive a reduced inventory of
most promising schemes for possible implementtion or preparation during the planning period.
Since circumst     will undoubtedly change over this period with a shifing focus of bydro
development, this inventory should be a long list rather than a short list. The inventory and
associated potential demands in the areas served should be prepared in tabular, graphic and data
base formats for reference by the various managerial and planning persomel who will need to
access and update the data over time.
11.       In presentation of this inventory the consultant should indicate which schemes
would be candidates for fast track implementtion, short-cting the long gestation period of
hydroelectric schemes. Very promising schemes at inventory/reconnaissance level could be
slated to go directly to feasibility study. Relatively straightforward schemes with limited cost,
low geotechmical uncertainy, and limited envrnmetlresettlement impacts could be slated for
design/construct implementation from feasibility stage.
12.        Based on the above, PLN in consltation with the consultant will select a number
of schemes (tentatively estimated at 9) to be carried out by PLN personnel with the assistance
of the consultant over the remainder of the study period. As input to this selection process the
consultant will prepare, together with PLN, budgets for field investigations for prospective
schemes.

-90-                           ANNEX 5.6
NDONESIA
CIRATA HYDROELECTRIC PHASE II PROJECT
TERMS OF REFERENCE FOR ENVIROMNENTAL STuDIES AND TRAIIG
BACKGROUND
1.        The Ciratum river -s located in western Java and the three main reservoirs on the
Ciratum River are Saguling, Cirata and Jatiluhur. The downstream reservoir, Jatiluhur, was
developed in 1964, mainly for irrigation. With assistan  from the World Bank, PLN have
constucted the upstream reservoir, Saguing (completed 1985), and the niiddle reservoir, Cirata
(completed 1988). All three reservoirs are used to produce hydropower with the current
installed capacities being: Sagulig 7'0 MW, Cirata 500 MW and Jatiluhur 150 MW. Table 1
gives further technical details of the reservois, civil works and power station outputs.
2.         Cirata Phase II will double the instilled capacity the Cirata power station to 1000
MW and improve PLN's ability to med peak loads. Included in the Cirata Phase II project is
an enviromnental study of the Saguling/Cirata system. This environmental study has been
included because of concerns about the enviromnental condition of the PLN reservoirs on the
Ciratum River.
3.         Some 5 million people live in the cachens of the Ciratum River. They produce
large volumes of industial and domestic waste causing enviomenl problems in the reservoirs,
particularly Saguling where runoff from the Bandung area discharges into the reservoir. Other
rural land uses, such as forestry and agricuture, add to the sedimentation and nutrient loads.
PARTICULAR ENVIRON1MNTAL PROBLEM
4.         When PLN created the Saguling and Cirata reservoirs, many people were displaced.
In Saguling reservoir alone over 3000 families were diredy displaced from private land within
the flooded area and over 7000 families from outside the flooded area lost jobs. PLN adopted
floating net aquacutue (ffNCA) as one of the resettlement option for families affected by the
flooding. The FNCA program is proving to be saccessful with about 1600 families in Saguling
and 1300 families in Cirata currently using this technique. A smaller number of units are
operating in Jatiluhur. However, the reservoirs thermally stratify during the dry season and the
high organic loading cases anoxic conditions in the lower levels. With the onset of the wet
season the reservoirs de-stratify causing fish deaths. At present the fish deaths are at a tolerable
level, being seen by the fish farmers as an acceptable risk, but reservoir pollution conditions
could worsen and make the risk intolerable.
5.         The industrial pollution entering Saguling reservoir from the Bandung area contains
high levels of some heavy metals. This pollution is a threat to the aquaculture industry and also
to the large downstream population that relies on the Ciratum River for drnkig and irrigation
water. Aquatic weeds and floating garbage also cause problems and PLN expends much money
and effort in the regular removal of both the floating aquatic weeds and the garbage.



-91-                            ANN-  5.6
OBJECTIVES
6.         The objectives of this study are:
-    to investigate the envionena conditions of the catom   and reseroirs of the
Cirata and Sagling power developments;
-    to gain a beuer knowledge of the pollutant sources;
-    to undemtand how the various pollutants affect the reservoirs.
-    to identfy options to improve the envonmetal health of the existing P1LN
reservoirs on the Ciratun River.
-    to assist the Government of Indonesia and PLN to improve the environmental
management of the Ciratum River reservoirs.
SCOPE OF WORKS
Data Collection
7.         ExItin  Data. The consutants will collect, review and evaluate the existng
enviromental data reladng to the Ciraum River reservoirs. This includes physical, chemical and
biological water quality samples, sediment data, information on water weed and garbage
clearance, erosik or "critie-l" land areas, re-forestation areas, fish deaths, fish production,
hydrologic data, tustrial effluent levels, population statistics, land use, cdlmatic and any other
appropriate data. rhe evaluation shoudd include:
who collected the data, where it is held, how it is archived and its quality and/or
relevance;
-    an evaluation of sampling procedures and laboratory testing;
-    an analysis of trends in the environmental data;
-    a preliminary mass balance of the important pollutants and nutrients showing what
proportion of each major pollutant is contributed by the various tributaries or sub-
-    comparison of the data for the Ciratum Reservoirs with pre-impoundment data and
data for similar reservoirs within Indonesia which do not suffer from the same
problems;
-    an assement of the various physical, chemical and biological processes taking
place in the reservoirs, sediments and inflow streams.
8.         Additional Data Collection. While the Ciratum River is recognised as a the
primary source of pollutant there is concemn about pollutant and sediments discharging directly
into the reservoir from the inhabited areas bordering the reservoir. The consultant will collect



-92-                            ANNEX 5.6
and evaluate data on direct discharge of pollutants from the periphery of the reservoirs. This will
include data on the population and industries involved, sewerage discharges, agricultural
practices, disposal of floating wastes, weed disposal and sediment input from land use practices.
9.         Revised Sampig Program. Following the review and evaluation of the data
collected the consultant will recommend a revised sampling program considering the need for:
-    the samplifg of some water quality parameters from the water surface to the bed
of the reservoir to obtain profiles and define strafication;
-    alterations to the range of water quality parameters tested;
-    increasing the sampling frequency of some parameters particularly those that can
be carried out by PLN staff based on site;
-    installation of additional streamflow/water quality stations on input streams to
improve knowledge of the amount and distribution of pollution and sediment,
partiularly upstream of Saguling;
-    installation of equipment to continuously record some water quality parameters at
streamflow stations;
-    sampling of lake sediments;
-    further fish pathology work to link FNCA fish deaths to stratification and water
quality changes;
-    how and where the data should be archived and what on-going evaluation is
required;
10.        Catelment and lpservoir Management. Following the data collection, review
and evaluation phase the consultant will make recommendations concerning:
-    measures which could be taken to mitigate the effects of the direct discharge of
pollutant and sediment into the reservoirs so that PLN can make representations to
the appropriate authorities;
-    appropriate measures for the disposal of floating waste and floating weeds;
-    power station operations and reservoir management practices to improve water
quality in the reservoirs.(e.g. aeration or spacing of fish farms);
11.        Staffing, Traing and Organizaton. While the scope of work described above
is related only to the environmenta managaement of PLN reservoirs, the review of staffing,
organization and training should cover environmental monitoring of all operating power plants
(both hydro and thermal). The Consultant will review exiting organizational arrangements,
procedures and staff capabilities, and in consultation with PLN:



-93-                           ANNEX 5.6
-    report on appropriate units in PLN to carry out routine, environmental monitoring,
interpretation and evaluation;
-    report on staffing necessary to carry out this work;
-    report on additional training of PLN staff, to enable them to effectively carry out
this work;
-    review existing procedures and mamals used for enviromiental monitoring and
assist with any upgrades required;
-    conduct seminars during the study and after the draft final report, to keep PLN
staff in touch with the findings of the study as they unfold.
12.          quipme Requments. The consultant will make recommendations on PLN
equipment requirements necessary to upgrade its monitoring programs.
Impemetionl
13.        Following acceptance of the recommendations by PLN the consultant will:
-    procure equipment using IBRD international shopping procedures;
-    manage the technical program for a two year period;
-    provide and calibrate a mathematical model to assess the biological assimilative
capacity of the reservoirs and to improve the understanding and prediction of
stratification and de-stratification of the reservoirs;
-    examine the factors leading to flih deaths during stratification and de-stratification.
-    carry out and/or manage any aspects of training which PLN entrusts to the
Consultant.
Reportng

*    recommendations concerning the revised sampling program, catchment and

WDONESA
CIRATA HYDROELECTRIC PHASE II PROJECT
TRAING iN INSTARumNTATiON MONTORING AND DAM SATY INsPEcnONs
TEm  OF REFERINCE
Bacgound
1.         PLN has several dams which are categorized as large dms under the ICOLD
definition, including the 99 m high Saguling and 125 m high Cirata rockflll dams. For SaguWing
and Cirata dams PLN has been carrying out routine m tenance in accordance with procedre
manuals prepared by the designers, together with reguar monitoring of dam instr ion, and
head office review of monitoring reports. Also PLN's Engineeng Services Center (PPE) has
formed a group to cairy out dam safety inspections of all PLN's large dams. PLN has decided
to upgrade its dam safety inspection procedures by the establishment of a Dam Safety Panel of
international experts. This Panel would meet at one to two year intervals, at which time the PPE
group would present a report on their inspections and monitoring in the intervening period, for
the Panel's review. The Panel would inspect the larger dams and any others where PLN
monitoring had revealed problems. In between meetngs, the Panel could also be consulted,
collectively or individually, to advise PLN staff if and when the need arose.
2.         To enable PLN staff to fulfil their role in this program, PLN has decided to employ
consultants to train them in the areas of intumentaton monitoring and dam safety inspections.
Objectives
3.         The objective of the tining program is to upgrade the skills of PLN staff to the
stage where they are fully capable of carrying out instrumentation monitoring and dam safety
inspections, interpreting the resuts thereon and acing on the results. They should also be
trained in preparing reports, including compilation and interpretation of data, and reports suitable
for review by the Dam Safety Panel. PLN staff involved would include the Operations personnel
responsible for the routine monitoring and inspection, and PPE staff responsible for review,
interpretation and acting of results.
Scope of Work
Ilstrumentato for Dams
4.         The Consultant should conduct courses in the various aspects of instrumentation for
both concrete and embnkmn includig:
(a) DesgnM Aspects: The purpose of various types of instrumnentation, ype of
instrumts  which may be selected for a particular purpose; advantages and



-96-                           A EX 5.7
disadvantages of various types; typical nstrume ltationlyouts for embankment and
concrete dams;
(b)  Practical Aspects of           oo  Monitorig:  Frequency of readings
methodological considerations, etc;
(c)   Compiladoa and Reporting of Results: Particular attendon should be given to
computerized data base compilation, and the preparation of reports from the data
base to facilitate comparison and interpretation of results;
(d)  Intepetation of Instruentation Results: Nonnal behavior, umnu  behavior;
likely instrumentation response to typical problems; etc.
S.         The courses should be complemented by practical reference to Sagding and Cirata
instrumentation. For example, the reasoning (actual if known, or otherwise probable) behind
the instrumentation layout for Saguling and Cirata, and the types of defects that intu aion
might be expected to detect; drawing attention to any shortcomings in instumentaon monitoring
at the two dams; setting up a computerized data base and reporting system to be used for
compiation of all future results; and interpreting of results to date including identification and
explantion for anomalous readings.
Daw Safety Inspections:
6.         The Consultant should conduct courses in Dam Safety Inspections, including the
following aspects:
-    dam safety programs of various operating authorities worldwide;
-    designing inspction programs using as an example PLN dams without existing
inspection program;
-    practical aspects of dam safety inspections;
-    compilation of inspection data, and reporting of results.
7.         These courses should also be complemented by practical reference to maintenance
and inspection programs of Saguling and Cirata, but also by design of inspection programs and
carfying out of inspections at other PLN dams.
Overseas Training
8.        It is expected that the training would generally be conducted in Indonesia.
However, an overseas study tour is also envisaged, to visit operating authorities and review their
dam safety inspection programs. Preferably, this should cover about three authorities, who could
be regarded as leaders in the field, and who are representative of the range of approaches to this
subject.

isues
5.        The main issues that need to be addressed are as follows:
(a) What steps are needed to raise the internal financing capacity of PLN?
How can the electricity prices be mainained at a level that permit PLN to
produce about 30 percent self-financing capability? What is the role of
semi-automatic formula based taiffl adjustment mechanism to ensure that
electricity prices are adjusted when factors such as fuel pnces, and inflation,
affect the input costs?
(b)  What is the level of Govermuent Equity that should be provided to PLN?
How will such level be determed? What mechansm needs to be put in
place to predict equity contribution over a five-year period so that
investment planing can take place? Should PLN be required to pay
dividend on equity and if so, at what rate?
(c)  What would be the principal sources of borrowings? Would official sources
be able to provide the projected funding requirements? If not, how will
commercial borrowing be intoduced in the sector? Should PLN's be
required to take the foreign exchange isk on the foreign borrowings?
(d) Is there a need to prepare local capital markets to participate in the power
investments through PLN floaing bonds? What are the requirements of the
capital markets to perceive PLN to be a creditworthy institon? What
steps will PLN need to take to iprove its financial management and
accounting system to meet the standard of the commercial lenders?
(e)  Should the Government contiue to guantee aU the borrowings by PLN?
Could PLN be prepared to access local and intemational capital markets?
If so, what steps wiUl be needed to make PLN a more commercially oriented
utility?

INDONESIA
CIRATA HYDROELECTRIC PHASE II PROJECT
ftOJP  r SUPERVISON ARRANGEMEN
1.           PL.N's Superviion I-. PLN has appointed a project manager for
the construction of the hydroelectric plant, and a manager for each related contract. The project
manager will submit by facsimile a brief monthly progress report, supplemented by more
detailed quarterly reports on the physical progress of the project. Constructon will be
supervised by an international consulting engineering firm with local associates. Technical
assitance components, other than those for policy support studies to be carried out by GO0, will
be managed by PLN's Engineering Service Center (PPE) who will be supervising the work of
the consultants.
2.           GOr's Superviion Arnge. Technical assistance components for policy
support studies will be managed by the Directorate General of Electricity and Energy
Development (DGEED), Ministry of Mines and Energy, which will be superisming the work of
consultants.
3.           Bank SupervisIon In4uts. During the initial phase of project implemention,
Bank supervsion will involve close monitoring of the procument and technical assistance
activities. Preonm uon engineei  services for the Besai hydroelectric scheme will be
momtored as part of the preparaion of a future Bank lending operation that would include that
project as a component. Work on policy support studies to be carried out by 001 will be
followed up as part of the sector dialogue with GOI.
4.           The Bank staff resources to supervise the project would be about ten staff weeks
per year for the first two years of project implementation, and would require about three
supervision missions each year; thereafter Bank staff resource requirements would decline
gradually to about six staff weeks and two supervision missions per year. Most of the field
supervision activities would be carried out in conjunction with the supervision of other ongoing
power projects and the processing of future lending operations.

-113-                          ANNEX 6.2
WDONESIA
CIRATA HYDROELECTRIC PHASE II PROJECT
NOTEs AND ASSUMPTONS FOR TE FINANCIAL FORECArSt
1.        Inflation has been taken into account as follows:
(a)  for foreign costs (including indirect foreign costs, excluding fuels), 2.1 percent per
annum in 1991, 4.3 percent per annum in 1992, and 3.1 percent per annum in 1993
and each year thereafter; and
(b) for local costs (excluding indirect foreign costs and fuels) at 9.4 percent per annum
in 1991, 6 percent per annum in 1992, and 5.5 percent per annum in 1993 and each
year thereafter.
2.         Separation of accounts of PLN's operations In Java and outside Java is based
on individual accounts mainained by PLN for each of its regional units. These accounts are
separate except for overhead expenses, which, for the purposes of financial projections, have
been allocated proportionately on the basis of unit sales of energy.
3.        Eneg sales are based on the sales forecasts in Annexes 2.3 and 2.4.
4.         Revemues for FY92/93 are based on the estimated average revenue per unit sold
in that year. The tariff was increased by 13 percent in FY92/93, effective February 1, 1993.
5.         Fue/purchased power costs for FY92/93 are based on the estmated outcome for
that year, and for FY93/94 and beyond are derived from PLN's computer simulation of system
operation. The fuel costs are calculated on the basis of current domesdc fuel prices (established
in January 1993) charged to PLN: fuel oil - Rp 240/1 plus tranWortation charges of about
Rp 24/1 for Java and about Rp 34/1 for Outside Java; diesel - Rp 380/1 plus transportation
charges of about Rp 27/M for Java and about Rp 32/1 for Outside Java; coal - Rp 60/kg for Java
and Rp 53.8/kg for Outside Java; geothermal - Rp 49.28/kWh for plants at Kamojang, Dieng
and Outside Java, and US$0.047/kWh for plants at Salak and Drajat; and natural gas -
US$2.53/thousand standard cubic feet (MSCF) for Java and US$3.00/MSCF for Outside Java.
The fuel prices to PLN are escalated on the assumption that prices for fuel oil, diesel and coal
will change in line with intermational prices and the prices for natural gas and geothermal will
remain constant in dollar terms. Purchase of power from private investors who would constuct
generating plant in Indonesia on a "Build, Own and Operate" (BOO) basis, which is projected
to commence from 1996/97, is assumed to be at Rp 115/kWh (in 1991/92 prices).
6.         Operatig expenses, other than fuel and persomel expenses, have been forecast
taking into account the facilities expected to be in use each year, and subject to foreign and local
cost inflation, as appropriate. Personnel expenses are estimated to increase about 18 percent per
annm to provide for inflation, volume growth and staff training. Repair and maintenance



-114-                         ANNEX 6.2
expenses for PLN's Java and outside-Java operations are estimated at 2.3 percent and 2.8 percent
of their respective gross fixed assets in operation during the preceding fiscal year.
7.        Depreciation is based on the application of PLN's normal rates (straight-line
method) to assets revalued annually. For purposes of corporate icome tax, the current
Indonesian tax code permits the use of the accelerated method of depreciation of assets in
operation instead of the straight-line method.
8.        Financing. Terms of foreign borrowings are assumed to be 20 years includig 5
years' grace, at average interest rates of 13.5 percent per annam. Terms of local borrowings
are assumed to be 12 years including two years' grace, at interest rates of 22 percent per annum;
local bonds are assumed to be issued at similar interest rates with a maturity of five years.
9.        Capita expendres are based on PLN's investment program (Annexes 2.5 and
2.6), and exclude capital expenditures for generation projects proposed to be implemented by
private investors, and the costs of geothermal steam field development, which are assumed to
be incurred by other agencies. The 10 percent Value Added Tax (VAT) on the foreign contracts
is assumed to be borne by Government, and on the local contracts it is assumed to by borne by
PLN.
10.       Govemment equity contributions are assumed to be equal to 24 percent of PLN's
capital expenditures in 1993/94, and to 15 percent of annual capital expenditures from 1994/95
onwards.
11.       Gross fixed assets, work in progress and consumers'   buons are
revalued annually, up to FY86/87 according to Government Regulation No. 45/1986, dated
October 2, 1986, and from FY87/88 onwards, according to weighted anmual escalation rates
(foreign and local). The rate base is the average of the revalued net fixed assets in operation,
less revalued consumers' contributions. The rate base does not include work in progress.
12.       Customer accounts recevables are assumed at about 37 days of energy revenues,
which is the current level.
13.       TransmIon and Distibutien (T&D) system losses are estimated to be about
14.5 percent for Java and 16.5 percent for outside Java in FY92/93, and are assumed to decline
gradually thereafter to about 11.5 percent and 14.5 percet, respectively in FY98/99.
14.       SubstItion of fid ofl in the existing oil-fired Units 3 and 4 at Gresik in Java by
natural gas is assumed to be completed during FY93/94.
15.       All projections are net of Corporate tax. Based on the current Indonesian tax code,
PLN does not expect to pay any corporate income tax during the projection period.
16.       By the Minister of Finance decree No. 1232/KMK.013/1989 dated November 11,
1989, PLN is required to contrbute to a fund for the Development of Cooperatves and Weak
Economic Businesses (DCWEB) an amount equal to 2.5 percent of its annual net income after
corporate tax. Such contibutions for DCWEB commenced from 1991/92.



-115-                          ANNEX 6.2
17.       In te   of Government Regulation No. 17 of 1990, PLN is requhred to contribute
to an Owerall Development Fod (DPS), 55 perent of its anual net income after contribution
for DCWEB (pan. 15) and corporate tax. Contnrbuons to DPS due for 1990/91 onwards are
assmed to be paid in five equa installmns commening two years after the year for which the
payment to DPS is due.

-123-                      ANNEX 7.3
INDONESA
CIRATA HYDROELECTC PHASE II PROJECT
MERR CALCmLTION: BENEm  MTODOLOGY FOR
REsmEnmuA CONSUMERS
1.          Tbe methodology used to estimte economic benefits for residentil
consumer can be eWlained with the help of Figure 1, which shows a demand fuiction
for a household defined by two points. One end of the function is anchored by the
prie-quantity pair (Q1,P,), where Q1 denotes household consumption of electicity at
PLN's average price, PI, to this consumer class. To anchor the "upper end" of the
demand fnction, which represents consumer willingness to pay for higher valued uses
of electricity, the prie-quantity pair (Q0,P0) is esmated am   that kerosene would
be used for lighig absent availabiLity of electricity. Household survey data on
kerosene consumption and expenditure for lighting and other data were udlized to
establish the monthly kWh consumption (Qo) that would yield an equivalent amount of
lightg intensity (lumens) prior to the availability of electricity; as well as to convert
the unit financial price of kerosene to an equivalent financial cost per kWh of lighting
(Pk). Thus, prior to electification,   units of kerosene (in kWh equivalence) are
consmed at a financial price of Pk. Following elctrification, Q, units of electricity
are consumned at a price of PI.
2.          The net econmic benefit associated with released resourmes is estimated
as (P-PIC)QO, where Pk represents the economic price of kerosene per kWh of
equivalent lighting, PI, is the eonomic cost of electricity supply, and QD is defined
above.
3.          The inremental consumer surplus on the diverted market is represented
by the shaded area in Figure 1. The precise shape of the demad function between the
points (Q,,P,), and (QO,Pk) is not known and can be postulated to be linear or a
suitable non-linear functional form such as a constant elasticity of substitution (CES)
demand function. Either specification wiUl tend to underestmte the wilinns to pay
since neither accounts for the additional value of the higher quality of light obtained
from electricity. Nevertheless, benefits have been esdmated as 50 percent of the
sbaded area assuming a linear demand function. The resultant surplus amount turns
out to be approximately equal to an alternate estimate of the shaded area using a CES
demand function. The implied price elasticity at the point (Q1,P1) was found to be
-0.4. Available evidence in Indonesia suggests that the price elasticity of electricity
at the low levels of consumption is in the range of -0.2 to -0.45. This implies that
demand is more inelastic than assumed in the benefits calculation, or equivalently that
the wvillngnes to pay is likely to be higher than esdmated.
